مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوتا اس لئے دس ذراع اور نو ذراع پر جو نصف ذراع زائد ہے اس کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں ہوگا بلکہ مشتری ساڑھے دس ذراع کی صورت میں اس تھان کو دس درہم کے عوض لے لے اور ساڑھے نو ذراع کی صورت میں اس کو نو درہم کے عوض لے لے اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کسی نے کوئی چیز عیب دار ہونے کی شرط کے ساتھ خریدی پھر وہ نکلی سلیم تو اس وصف سلامت اور جودت کے مقابلے میں کوئی ثمن نہیں ہوگا بلکہ یہ وصف مشتری کے لئے مفت ہوگا۔ زیادتی کی صورت میں مشتری کو اختیار اس لئے نہ ہوگا کہ اس صورت میں مشتری کو نصف ذراع زائد حاصل ہوا ہے اور ثمن کی زیادتی کا ضرر لاحق نہیں ہوا۔ اور نقصان کی صورت میں چونکہ صفقہ بدل گیا اس لئے نقصان کی صورت میں مشتری کو خیار حاصل ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ تینوں اقوال اس کپڑے میں ہیں جس کپڑے کی جوانب متفاوت ہوتی ہیں لیکن وہ کپڑا جس کی جوانب متفاوت نہیں ہوتیں اگر وہ کچھ زائد نکلا تو زیادتی مشتری کے لئے حلال نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ زائد بائع کو واپس کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایسا کپڑا کیلی اور وزنی چیزوں کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ چنانچہ اس کو جدا کر لینا کوئی مضر نہیں ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے مشائخ نے کہا کہ اس قسم کے تھان میں سے ایک ذراع کا بیچنا جائز ہے یعنی جس طرح اناج کے ڈھیر میں سے ایک قفیز کی بیع جائز ہے اسی طرح اس قسم کے تھان میں سے
If someone buys a cloth on condition that it is ten yards, each yard for one dirham, then it turns out to be ten and a half yards or nine and a half yards: Abu Hanifa said: In the first case, he takes it for ten dirhams without option; in the second case, he takes it for nine dirhams if he wishes. Abu Yusuf said: In the first case, he takes it for eleven dirhams if he wishes; in the second, for ten dirhams if he wishes. Muhammad said: In the first, for ten and a half dirhams if he wishes; in the second, for nine and a half dirhams, and he has the option because of the necessity of matching half a yard with half a dirham, so the ruling applies to the half. Abu Yusuf's proof: Since he specified a price for each yard, each yard becomes like a separate cloth, and it has decreased. Abu Hanifa's proof: The yard is originally a description, and the ruling of quantity was taken by condition, which is tied to the yard, so when the condition is absent, the ruling returns to the original. It is said regarding thick cloth whose sides do not differ: the excess over the conditioned amount is not permissible for the buyer because it is like a weighed item, where separation does not harm it. Based on this, they said selling a yard from it is permissible. Then the commentary explains the positions and proofs in detail, and concludes that these three opinions apply to cloth with varying sides, but for cloth with uniform sides, the excess is not permissible for the buyer; it must be returned to the seller.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale of cloth where the buyer conditions that the cloth is ten yards, each yard for one dirham, but the actual measurement is either 10.5 or 9.5 yards. The three principal imams differ on the price adjustment and whether the buyer has an option (khiyar). Abu Hanifa holds that if the cloth is longer, the buyer takes it for the agreed ten dirhams without option; if shorter, he takes it for nine dirhams with the option to reject. Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer takes it for eleven dirhams (if longer) or ten dirhams (if shorter), with option in both cases. Muhammad holds that the price is adjusted proportionally: 10.5 dirhams for longer, 9.5 for shorter, with option in both cases. The commentary then provides the proofs: Muhammad relies on proportional division of the yard into parts, Abu Yusuf treats each yard as a separate commodity, and Abu Hanifa views the yard as a description that reverts to its original nature when the condition is unfulfilled. The author, Sahib al-Hidayah, concludes that these three opinions apply only to cloth with varying quality; for uniform cloth (like thick cloth where sides are equal), the excess cannot be taken by the buyer—it must be returned to the seller, because such cloth is treated like weighed goods (mawzun), where separation does not harm the contract. This is why scholars permit selling a single yard from such cloth, similar to selling a qafiz from a heap of grain.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A unit of length, cubit; in this context, it refers to the yard of cloth.
شرط (shart): Condition; the buyer stipulates that the cloth is exactly ten yards.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the consideration paid for the cloth.
صفقة (safqah): The transaction or deal; here refers to the unity of the contract.
موزون (mawzun): Weighed items; in this context, uniform cloth is likened to weighed goods where separation does not affect the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hidayah, if a cloth is uniform (sides do not differ) and the measurement exceeds the conditioned amount, what must the buyer do with the excess?
A) Pay proportional price for the excess and keep it.
B) Return the excess to the seller.
C) Reject the entire sale.
D) Keep the excess for free.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on yardage variance is restricted to non-uniform cloth; for uniform cloth, excess must be returned to the seller because it is treated as weighed goods.

The Buyer's Option (Khiyar) When Cloth Length Varies: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's option when cloth is more or less than specified yards

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad regarding the buyer's khiyar when cloth sold by the yard is more or less than specified, and explain the resolution given in Al-Hidayah.

Core Idea
A buyer's option when cloth length varies depends on whether the yard is a description or a principal, and the type of cloth.

This passage presents a sophisticated intra-Hanafi dispute on a practical sale scenario: a buyer orders 10 yards of cloth at 1 dirham per yard but receives either 10.5 or 9.5 yards. Does the buyer have an option (khiyar) to cancel the sale? The three leading Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—each offer a distinct reasoning based on how they classify the 'yard' (dira') as a description (wasf) or a principal (asl). Understanding their arguments reveals the deeper usul principles that shape Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
Recognize that the dispute centers on whether the buyer has khiyar when the actual cloth length differs from the specified number of yards, and how price adjustments are handled.
The scenario assumes a sale 'kullu dira' bi dirham' (each yard for a dirham).
2
Analyze Imam Muhammad's Proportional Approach
Imam Muhammad views the yard as divisible; price is proportional to the actual length. Thus both excess and deficiency trigger a division of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah), giving the buyer khiyar.
This treats the sale as many mini-sales per part of a yard.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Yusuf's Principal Approach
Imam Abu Yusuf treats each full yard as a principal (asl), so the full price is due per yard regardless of fraction. In excess, the buyer gains half a yard but pays an extra dirham (harm), giving option; in deficiency, the description is lost, also giving option.
The yard here is like an individual item; half-yards are not recognized.
4
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's Descriptive Approach
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the yard is originally a description (wasf); the condition 'kullu dira' bi dirham' makes it a principal only for full yards. Fractions revert to description, which is free. So excess is free (no harm, no option), deficiency changes the transaction (option exists).
This limits khiyar to deficiency only.
5
Examine Al-Hidayah's Reconciliation
The author of Al-Hidayah notes that these three opinions apply only to cloth with varying sides (jawanib mutafawwitah). For uniform cloth, any excess must be returned; it is akin to measured goods. This becomes the mufta bihi (operative ruling).
This shows how later Hanafi scholarship can restrict the scope of an ikhtilaf.

مشتری کو نصف ذراع زائد حاصل ہوا ہے اور شمن کی زیادتی کا ضر ر لاحق نہیں ہوا۔ اور نقصان کی صورت میں چونکہ صفقہ بدل گیا اس لئے نقصان کی صورت میں مشتری کو خیار حاصل ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مذکورہ تینوں اقوال اس کپڑے میں ہیں جس کپڑے کی جوانب متفاوت ہوتی ہیں لیکن وہ کپڑا جس کی جوانب متفاوت نہیں ہوتیں اگر وہ کچھ زائد نکلا تو زیادتی مشتری کے لئے حلال نہیں ہے بلکہ وہ زائد بائع کو واپس کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ایسا کپڑا کیلی اور وزنی چیزوں کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ چنانچہ اس کو جدا کر لینا کوئی مضر نہیں ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے مشائخ نے کہا کہ اس قسم کے تھان میں سے ایک ذراع کا بیچنا جائز ہے یعنی جس طرح اناج کے ڈھیر میں سے ایک قفیز کی بیع جائز ہے اسی طرح اس قسم کے تھان میں سے ایک ذراع کا بیچنا جائز ہے۔ اگر چہ اس کی جگہ متعین نہ کی ہو ۔ جمیل احمد عفی عنہ ۔
Imam Muhammad's proof is that when one dirham is in exchange for one yard, then for every part of the yard, there will be a corresponding part of the dirham. For example, in exchange for half a yard, there will be half a dirham, and in exchange for a quarter yard, a quarter dirham, and for an eighth part, an eighth part of a dirham. So when the rule of exchange applies to parts of the yard, then in case of excess, ten and a half yards will require ten and a half dirhams. And in case of deficiency, nine and a half yards will require nine and a half dirhams. And since in case of excess the transaction is divided, the buyer also has an option, as previously passed, in the case of division of the transaction the buyer has the option to take or not. The proof of Imam Abu Yusuf is that when he said 'dira' bi dirham' and mentioned the price in exchange for each yard, then 'dira' is no longer a description but became the principal. And ten yards become like ten pieces of cloth, so in case of excess it is as if the buyer bought eleven pieces but the eleventh piece came out short, and in case of deficiency it is as if he bought ten pieces but the tenth piece came out short. And due to the decrease in the yard, the price is not reduced, so the price of the full yard is obligatory, that is, in the first case eleven dirhams are obligatory, and in the second case ten dirhams are obligatory. And the buyer has khiyar because in case of excess, although the buyer gained half a yard of profit, that profit is mixed with the harm of an extra dirham. So to avoid this harm, the buyer has the option to cancel the sale. And in case of deficiency, because the desired description is lost, the buyer has khiyar. For example, someone bought a slave on the condition that he is a cook, then it turned out he is not a cook; the buyer has the option to cancel the sale. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that it has already been established that 'dira' is originally a description; no part of the price comes in exchange for it. But with the condition 'kullu dira' bi dirham' he made it a principal. And the condition is restricted to the yard, and since half a yard is not a yard, the condition ceases to exist in half a yard. And when the condition ceases, the ruling returns to its original, i.e., description, so half a yard will be called a description, not a principal, and no price is paid for a description. Therefore, for the extra half yard beyond ten or nine yards, no price is due, but rather in the case of ten and a half yards, the buyer takes that piece for ten dirhams, and in the case of nine and a half yards, he takes it for nine dirhams. This is like someone buying a defective item on condition, then it turns out sound; no price is for the description of soundness and quality; rather this description is free for the buyer. In case of excess, the buyer does not have khiyar because he gained half a yard extra and was not harmed by an increase in price. But in case of deficiency, since the transaction changed, the buyer has khiyar. The author of Hidayah says that these three opinions apply to cloth whose sides vary. But if the cloth's sides do not vary, and it is found to be extra, that extra is not lawful for the buyer; rather it must be returned to the seller. Because such cloth is like measured and weighed goods. Cutting it separately is not harmful. For this reason, the Mashayikh said that selling one yard from such a piece is permissible, just as selling one qafiz from a heap of grain is permissible, even if its specific location is not determined. Jamil Ahmad 'ufiya 'anhu.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the buyer's option (khiyar) when cloth sold by the yard (dira' bi dirham) is found to be more or less than the specified length. The passage presents three proofs: from Imam Muhammad, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Abu Hanifa. Imam Muhammad argues that since the price is per yard, any increase or decrease in length proportionally changes the price; the buyer has option in both cases because the transaction is divided. Imam Abu Yusuf argues that 'dira' bi dirham' makes the yard a principal, so the buyer must pay the full price for each yard; in excess he gains a half-yard but pays an extra dirham, causing harm, so he has option; in deficiency, the description is lost, giving option. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that originally 'dira' is a description; the condition 'kullu dira' bi dirham' temporarily makes it a principal but only for full yards; half-yards revert to being descriptions, for which no price is due. Thus in excess, the buyer gets the extra half-yard for free and has no harm, so no option; in deficiency, the transaction changes, giving option. The author of Al-Hidayah notes that these three opinions apply only to cloth with varying sides (jawanib mutafawwit); for cloth with uniform sides, any excess must be returned to the seller, as it resembles measured goods. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the distinction between the two types of cloth.

Key Terms
ذراع (dira'): A unit of length, here meaning a yard; in this context, it can be a description (wasf) or a principal (asl) depending on the condition.
خیار (khiyar): The option to cancel or confirm the sale due to a defect, condition, or change in the transaction.
صفقہ (safqah): The transaction or deal; dividing the safqah means the sale becomes split into multiple parts, giving the buyer option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for what type of cloth does the Hidayah state that the excess must be returned to the seller?
A) Cloth with varying sides
B) Cloth with uniform sides
C) Any type of cloth
D) Only silk cloth

Key Takeaway
The buyer's khiyar in a sale by the yard depends on how the yard is classified (description vs. principal) and the uniformity of the cloth, with Al-Hidayah restricting the ikhtilaf to cloth with varying sides.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Automatic Inclusion of Buildings and Trees in Sale of Property
Rulings on automatic inclusion of structures and trees in property sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what items are automatically included in the sale of a house (dar) and land (ard), and apply the principles of custom (urf) and permanent attachment (ittisal qarar) to distinguish between included and excluded items.

Core Idea
In Hanafi sales, anything permanently attached is automatically included unless excluded by contract.

This passage from Kitab al-Buyu' of Al-Hidayah explains the important Hanafi principle that certain items are automatically included in the sale of real estate even if not mentioned. The key is whether the item is permanently attached to the property (ittisal qarar) or only temporarily attached (ittisal fasl). Additionally, the custom (urf) of Arabic speakers defines the scope of terms like 'dar' (house) to include its courtyard and building. Understanding these rules prevents disputes and ensures the contract reflects the actual intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two case studies
The passage provides two distinct scenarios: sale of a house (dar) and sale of land (ard). For each, the ruling on inclusion of attached items is given. Recognize that the ruling differs for buildings/trees versus crops.
Dar includes courtyard and building; ard includes trees but not crops.
2
Understand the governing principles
Two principles underpin the rulings: (1) Custom (urf) — the word 'dar' customarily includes the building. (2) Permanent attachment (ittisal qarar) — anything attached for permanence becomes a subordinate (tabi') of the sold item and is included. Temporary attachment (ittisal fasl) like crops keeps the item separate.
These principles are derived from the text: 'لان اسم الدار يتناول العرصة والبناء في العرف' and 'لانه متصل به للقرار'.
3
Apply the principles to each case
For dar: building is permanently attached and custom includes it. For ard: trees are permanently rooted, so they are like buildings and included. Crops are attached only for harvesting (temporary), so they are like movable goods and excluded unless mentioned.
The analogy (qiyas) is drawn: trees are compared to building; crops are compared to goods.

Which things enter into the sale without being mentioned by name?
Book of Sales
Section: And whoever sold a house (dar), its building enters into the sale even if he did not mention it, because the name 'dar' includes the courtyard and building in custom, and because it is attached to it with a permanent attachment, so it is a follower.
Translation: And whoever sold a house (dar), its building is included in the sale even if he did not mention it. Because in custom, the word 'dar' includes the courtyard and building. And because the building is attached to the land for permanence, so the building is subordinate to the courtyard and land. Explanation: In Urdu, the word 'dar' is used absolutely for house and home, and in this meaning 'manzil' and 'bayt' are used. But in Arabic there is a difference. So 'dar' is called that which has living quarters, small rooms, a front platform, kitchen, and stable. 'Manzil' is that which has small rooms, kitchen, and toilet, but no courtyard. 'Bayt' is a roofed structure with one door. (Hashiya Hidayah by reference to Siqaya)
The issues of this section are based on two principles:
(1) Everything that the custom of the word includes in the sale enters into the sale, even if not explicitly mentioned.
(2) Everything that is attached to the sold item for permanence becomes subordinate and enters the sale.
Now the case is: if a person sold a dar, its building enters the sale even if the building is not explicitly mentioned, because in custom the word 'dar' includes both courtyard and building. The second proof is that the building is attached to the land for permanence and continuation, so the building is subordinate to the courtyard and enters the sale. By selling land, date palms and other trees are included in the sale, except crops. And whoever sold land, what is in it of date palms and trees enters even if he did not mention them, because they are attached to it for permanence, so they resemble building. And crops do not enter into the sale of land except by explicit mention, because they are attached for separation, so they resemble the goods placed in it.
Translation: And whoever sold land, the date palms and other trees in it are included even if he did not mention them, because they are attached to the land for permanence, so they resemble building. And crops are not included in the sale of land except with explicit mention, because they are attached for separation, so they resemble the goods placed in it.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a ruling section (fasl) from Kitab al-Buyu', explaining what items are automatically included in the sale of a house or land without explicit mention. The commentary begins by clarifying the meaning of 'dar' in Arabic and Urdu contexts, noting that in Arabic, 'dar' specifically refers to a house with courtyard, rooms, kitchen, and stable, whereas 'manzil' lacks a courtyard and 'bayt' is a single-room structure. The rulings are based on two principles: first, custom (urf) determines the scope of the word; second, anything permanently attached to the sold item (for stability) is included as a subordinate. Thus, when selling a house (dar), the building is included because custom includes it and because it is permanently attached to the land. Similarly, when selling land, all trees (including date palms) are included because they are rooted and permanent, analogous to a building. However, crops (cereals, vegetables) are not included unless explicitly mentioned because they are attached only temporarily for harvesting and are like movable goods. This highlights the Hanafi principle of taba'iyyah (subordination) based on permanence of attachment.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house consisting of a courtyard, living quarters, kitchen, and stable; includes both land and building.
تابع (tabi'): That which is subordinate and follows the principal item in sale; included automatically.
اتصال قرار (ittisal qarar): Permanent attachment intended for stability, as opposed to temporary attachment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why are crops not automatically included in the sale of land?
A) Because crops are not valuable.
B) Because crops are attached to the land for separation, not permanence.
C) Because crops belong to someone else.
D) Because the seller must explicitly exclude them.

Key Takeaway
Buildings and trees are automatically included in the sale of a house or land due to custom and permanent attachment; crops require explicit mention because they are temporarily attached.

Sale of a House Includes Its Building by Custom and Permanent Attachment
Ruling that building is included in house sale by custom and attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a building is included in the sale of a house (dar) even if not explicitly mentioned, and explain the two proofs: customary inclusion and permanent attachment.

Core Idea
When you sell a house, the building goes with it — custom and permanent connection make it a subordinate part.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Hanafi school on sales: the automatic inclusion of a building when a house (dar) is sold. The passage establishes that the sale covers both the land and the structure, relying on the customary meaning of 'dar' and the principle that permanently attached items are subordinate. This ruling has practical implications for real estate transactions and is based on two general legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The author states that if someone sells a house (dar), the building is included in the sale even if not explicitly mentioned. Recognize this as the operative ruling.
This is a default inclusion, not dependent on the parties' intent.
2
Recognize the first proof: custom (urf)
The word 'dar' in customary usage includes both the courtyard (land) and the building. This is a linguistic-customary argument.
Custom is a recognized source of law in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize the second proof: permanent attachment (ittisal qarar)
The building is connected to the land with the intention of permanence, so it becomes subordinate (tabi') to the land and is included as a fixture.
This principle applies to any item permanently attached to the sold property.
4
Understand the terminology
The commentary distinguishes 'dar' (house with courtyard), 'manzil' (house without courtyard), and 'bait' (single room). This affects the scope of the sale.
These distinctions are specific to Arabic; Urdu usage may differ.
5
Apply the general principles
The chapter is based on two principles: (1) Anything included by custom in the sale term is included; (2) Anything permanently attached is subordinate. Apply these to other cases.
These principles are used in other areas of sales law.

Section: Whoever sells a house, its building is included in the sale even if he does not mention it, because the word 'dar' includes the courtyard and the building in custom, and because it is connected to it with a permanent connection, so it becomes a follower of it. Translation: And the person who sold a house (dwelling), then its building is included in the sale even if he did not mention it. Explanation: In Urdu, the word 'dar' is used absolutely for a house and dwelling, and in this sense 'manzil' and 'bait' are used. But in Arabic there is a difference. Thus 'dar' is what has a dwelling, small rooms, a front platform, a kitchen, and a stable for animals. 'Manzil' is what has small rooms, a kitchen, and a toilet, but no courtyard. 'Bait' is a roofed structure with a single threshold. (Hashiya Hidayah by Saqaya) The issues of this chapter are based on two principles: (1) Everything that is included in the word 'bay' by custom is included in the sale, even if not explicitly mentioned. (2) Everything that is connected to the sold item for permanence is included in the sale as a subordinate. Now the case is: A person sold a house, so its building will be included in the sale even if the building was not explicitly mentioned. Because in custom, the word 'dar' includes both the courtyard and the building. The second proof is that the connection of the building to the land is for permanence and remaining, so the building, being subordinate to the courtyard, enters the sale.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of sales (Buyu') concerning the sale of a house (dar). The author states that if someone sells a house, the building is automatically included in the sale even if not explicitly mentioned. This is based on two principles: first, the customary meaning of 'dar' includes both the land (courtyard) and the structure; second, the building is permanently attached to the land, so it follows the land as a subordinate (tabi'). The commentary elaborates on the Arabic terminology: 'dar' refers to a house with a courtyard, 'manzil' has no courtyard, and 'bait' is a single roofed room. These distinctions are important for understanding the scope of the term. The two principles laid out here are general: anything included in the sale by custom, and anything permanently attached, becomes part of the sale. This is a straightforward ruling with no ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house that includes a courtyard (sahn), rooms, kitchen, and stable, as distinct from manzil and bait.
اتصال قرار (ittisal qarar): Permanent attachment intended for stability, as opposed to temporary attachment for separation.
تبع (tabi'): Something that follows the main sold item in the sale, being subordinate to it.

Trees Included, Crops Excluded in Land Sale (Hanafi Ruling)
Ruling with qiyas and objection refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding inclusion of trees and crops in the sale of land and explain the legal basis (illa) of permanent vs. temporary attachment.

Core Idea
Trees are included in land sale because permanent attachment; crops are excluded because attached for harvest.

In this passage from al-Hidayah, the author addresses a common scenario in real estate transactions: when land with trees or standing crops is sold, what is automatically transferred? The ruling distinguishes between permanent and temporary attachment, using qiyas with building and goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text classifies things attached to land into two types: trees (nakhil wa shajar) and crops (zar'). Each has a different ruling.
Note the Arabic terms: attāl (trees) and zar' (crops).
2
Determine the illa (effective cause) for inclusion
Trees are attached for permanence (ittisal li al-qarar) – they remain on the land indefinitely. Crops are attached for separation (ittisal li al-fasl) – they will be harvested.
The illa is the purpose of attachment, not mere physical connection.
3
Follow the qiyas
Trees are analogized to a building (which is included in a house sale) because both are permanently attached. Crops are analogized to movable goods (which are not included in a house sale) because both are intended for removal.
This qiyas is based on custom and legal reasoning.
4
Address the objection about the fetus
A potential counterexample is the fetus (haml), which is also attached for separation but is included in the sale of the mother. The text answers that humans cannot separate the fetus by their own power; it is a divine act. Crops and goods can be separated by human effort, so the analogy is invalid.
This shows the Hanafi method of refining the illa.
5
Apply the ruling to any land sale
In a contract of land sale, trees are automatically transferred to the buyer unless explicitly excluded. Crops remain with the seller unless explicitly included.
Practical implication: always specify crops in the contract if desired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن باع ارضا دخل ما فيها من النخل والشجر وان لم يسمه لانه متصل به للقرار فشبه البناء ولايد خل الزرع فى بيع الارض الا بالتسمية لانه متصل به للفصل فشابه متاع الذي فيه ترجمہ اور جس شخص نے کوئی زمین فروخت کی تو اس میں اس زمین کے کھجور کے درخت اور دوسرے درخت داخل ہوں گے اگر چران کو ذکر نہ کیا ہو۔ کیونکہ یہ درخت اس زمین کے ساتھ برقرار رہنے کے لئے متصل ہیں۔ پس عمارت کے مشابہ ہو گئے ۔ اور زمین کی بیع میں اس کی کھیتی داخل نہ ہو گی ۔ مگر صراحتہ ذکر کر دینے سے۔ کیونکہ کھیتی زمین کے ساتھ جدا ہونے کے واسطے متصل ہے پس اس سامان کے مشابہ ہوگئی جو اس میں رکھا ہوا ہو ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے زمین فروخت کی اور اس زمین میں درخت ہیں تو یہ درخت زمین کی بیچ میں داخل ہوں گے درخت خواہ بڑے ہوں یا چھوٹے پھل دار ہوں یا بغیر پھلوں کے صراحتہ ان کا ذکر کیا ہو یا نہ کیا ہو دلیل یہ ہے کہ درختوں کا اتصال زمین کے ساتھ قرار اور باقی رکھنے کے واسطے ہے نہ کہ جدا کرنے کے لئے پس درخت عمارت کے مشابہ ہو گئے ۔ لہذا جس طرح دار کی بیع میں عمارت داخل ہوتی ہے اسی طرح زمین کی بیچ میں درخت داخل ہو جائیں گے اگر چہ عقد بیج کے وقت ان کا ذکر نہ کیا ہو۔ اور اگر زمین کے اندر کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے تو زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی داخل نہ ہوگی ۔ ہاں اگر عقد بیع کے وقت زمین کے ساتھ کھیتی کا ذکر کر دیا ہو تو کھیتی داخل ہو جائے گی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کھیتی زمین کے ساتھ متصل ہوتی ہے جدا کرنے کے لئے نہ کہ باقی رکھنے کے لئے پس کھیتی اس سامان کے مشابہ ہوگئی جو مکان میں رکھا ہو۔ لہذا جس طرح مکان کی بیع میں سامان داخل نہیں ہوتا اس طرح زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی بھی داخل نہ ہوگی۔ ایک سوال اور اس کا جواب اگر یوں کیا جا کر مل مل کے ساتھ جدا ہونے کے واسطے متصل ہوتا ہے لیکن اس کے باوجود حمل ماں کی بیچ میں داخل ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ قاعدہ کے موافق داخل نہ ہونا چاہئیے ۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حمل کا جدا کرنا انسان کی قدرت میں نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا کام ہے۔ اور کھیتی اور سامان کا جدا کرنا آدمی کی وسعت میں ہے۔ اس لئے حمل کو کھیتی اور مکان کے سامان پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا ۔
And whoever sells land, enters into it whatever is in it of date palms and trees, even if he does not mention them, because they are attached to it for permanence, so they resemble a building. And crops are not included in the sale of land except by mention, because they are attached for separation, so they resemble the goods that are in it. Translation: And the person who sold any land, then the date palms and other trees in that land will be included even if he did not mention them. Because these trees are attached to the land for permanence, so they resemble a building. And the crop of the land will not be included in the sale of the land, except by explicit mention. Because the crop is attached for separation, so it resembles the goods that are placed in it. Explanation: The case is that a person sold land and there are trees in that land, then these trees will be included in the sale of the land, whether the trees are big or small, fruit-bearing or not, whether they were explicitly mentioned or not. The proof is that the attachment of trees to the land is for permanence, not for separation, so trees resemble a building. Therefore, just as the building is included in the sale of a house, similarly trees will be included in the sale of land even if they were not mentioned at the time of the contract. And if there is standing crop in the land, then the crop will not be included in the sale of the land. Yes, if at the time of the contract the crop was mentioned along with the land, then the crop will be included. The proof is that the crop is attached to the land for separation, not for permanence, so the crop resembles the goods placed in a house. Therefore, just as goods are not included in the sale of a house, similarly crop is not included in the sale of land. A question and its answer: If it is said that the fetus is attached for separation, yet the fetus is included in the sale of the mother. While according to the rule it should not be included. The answer is that separating the fetus is not within human power, but it is the work of Allah. And separating crop and goods is within human capacity. Therefore, it is not correct to analogize the fetus to crop and household goods.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This is a ruling on the sale of land. The matn states that trees are automatically included in the sale of land, while crops are not unless explicitly mentioned. The reason is that trees are permanently attached to the land (ittisal li al-qarar), analogous to a building, while crops are attached temporarily for harvest (ittisal li al-fasl), analogous to movable goods. The commentary reinforces that trees of any type are included. A potential objection is raised: if crops are excluded because they are for separation, then why is a fetus included in the sale of a pregnant animal? The answer is that humans cannot separate the fetus at will; it is a divine act, whereas crops can be harvested. Hence the analogy does not hold. This distinction is based on capacity of separation, which is unique to Hanafi reasoning in such cases.

Key Terms
اتصال للفصل (ittisal li al-fasl): Attachment intended for separation, as in crops that will be harvested.
زرع (zar'): Crops standing in the field, considered movable goods for legal purposes.
حمل (haml): Fetus in the womb, which is included in the sale of the mother despite being for separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because trees are mentioned in the Quran
D) Because trees are considered part of the land in all schools

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, permanently attached trees are automatically included in the sale of land, while crops attached for harvest are not unless explicitly mentioned.

Trees Included in Sale of Land
Statement of ruling that trees pass with land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on trees included in the sale of land and explain the qiyas proof that trees pass automatically with the land.

Core Idea
Trees attached to land for permanence are included in its sale by analogy to a building.

When you buy a piece of land, do the trees on it automatically become yours? Hanafi fiqh gives a clear answer: yes, because they are permanently attached and intended to stay, much like a building. This short passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah establishes the ruling and provides its proof via analogy (qiyas). Understanding this principle helps avoid disputes in property transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling exactly
The text says: if land is sold with trees, the trees are included in the sale whether big or small, fruit-bearing or not, mentioned or not. Identify this as the core ruling.
The ruling applies to all trees, not only fruit-bearing ones.
2
Identify the effective cause ('illa)
The text gives the reason: the attachment of trees to the land is for permanence and retention (ithbat lil-qarar wa al-baqa'), not for removal. This is the common trait that triggers the analogy.
Temporary attachments like crops or potted plants have a different 'illa.
3
Understand the original case (asl)
The original case is the sale of a house: a building is attached to the land for permanence and is included in the sale without explicit mention. This is established by custom and necessity.
The asl is a building; the far' is a tree.
4
Apply qiyas (analogy)
Because both the building and the tree share the 'illa of permanent attachment, the ruling from the asl (inclusion in sale) transfers to the far' (tree).
This is qiyas jaliy because the 'illa is clearly evident.
5
Note the scope and any exceptions
The ruling covers all trees regardless of size or fruit. But if the attachment is temporary (e.g., crops), they are not included because the 'illa is absent.
Hanafi law distinguishes based on the nature of attachment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
۴۸
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد هشتم
تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے زمین فروخت کی اور اس زمین میں درخت ہیں تو یہ درخت زمین کی بیچ میں داخل ہوں گے درخت خواہ بڑے ہوں یا چھوٹے پھل دار ہوں یا بغیر پھلوں کے صراحتہ ان کا ذکر کیا ہو یا نہ کیا ہو دلیل یہ ہے کہ درختوں کا اتصال زمین کے ساتھ قرار اور باقی رکھنے کے واسطے ہے نہ کہ جدا کرنے کے لئے پس درخت عمارت کے مشابہ ہو گئے ۔ لہذا جس طرح دار کی بیع میں عمارت داخل ہوتی ہے اسی طرح زمین کی بیچ میں درخت داخل ہو جائیں گے اگر چہ عقد بیج کے وقت ان کا ذکر نہ کیا ہو۔
Book of Sales. 48. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu Commentary on al-Hidayah Volume 8. Explanation of the issue: If a person sold land and there are trees on that land, then these trees are included in the sale of the land, whether the trees are big or small, fruit-bearing or without fruit, whether explicitly mentioned or not mentioned. The proof is that the attachment of trees to the land is for permanence and retention, not for separation. Thus trees resemble a building. Therefore, just as a building is included in the sale of a house, similarly trees will be included in the sale of land, even if they were not mentioned at the time of the contract of sale.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage delivers a clear ruling: when land is sold, any trees growing on it automatically pass to the buyer. The key legal principle is the nature of attachment. Trees are attached to the land for permanence (istiqrar wa al-baqa'), not for removal (al-qat'). This makes them analogous to a building (imarat). Just as a building is included in the sale of a house without explicit mention, by analogy (qiyas) trees are included in the sale of land. The ruling applies regardless of size, fruit, or explicit mention. The proof is rational (qiyas jaliy) based on the common effective cause ('illa) of permanent attachment.

Key Terms
اتصال (ittisal): The physical attachment of trees to land, which is permanent and intended for retention.
مشابہ (mushabih): Something legally analogous, here trees are compared to buildings due to the nature of attachment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only fruit-bearing trees are included.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, trees attached to land for permanence are automatically included in the sale of the land by analogy to buildings, regardless of size or fruit.

6 min
Crops Not Included in Sale of Land Unless Mentioned
Ruling with proof and rebuttal to a counter-analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on standing crops in land sale and distinguish between attachment for separation vs. retention.

Core Idea
Crops are not part of land sale because they are attached for removal, not permanence.

In Hanafi sales law, the inclusion of items attached to land depends on the purpose of attachment. While trees are included due to permanent attachment, standing crops are not. This lesson explores the ruling, its basis in analogy to movable goods, and a rebuttal to the counterexample of a fetus.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on crops in land sale
Identify that if land is sold without mentioning crops, the crops remain the seller's. Only if explicitly mentioned do they pass.
2
Locate the proof (qiyas)
The proof is analogy to goods in a house: crops are attached for harvesting (separation), not for staying, so they are movable goods.
Key distinction: ittisal li al-qat' vs. ittisal li al-baqa'.
3
Analyze the illa (effective cause)
The illa is 'attachment for separation'. This is a jaliy illa, extracted via tahqiq al-manat.
This illa applies to crops and house goods, not to trees.
4
Note the objector's argument
An objector analogizes to a fetus: it is also attached for separation yet included in sale of the mother.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response distinguishes based on human capability: fetus separation is beyond human power (from Allah), while crop separation is within human capacity. Thus the analogy fails (qiyas ma'a al-fariq).
This preserves consistency: the ruling depends on human ability to separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور اگر زمین کے اندر کھیتی کھڑی ہوئی ہے تو زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی داخل نہ ہوگی ۔ ہاں اگر عقد بیع کے وقت زمین کے ساتھ کھیتی کا ذکر کر دیا ہو تو کھیتی داخل ہو جائے گی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کھیتی زمین کے ساتھ متصل ہوتی ہے جدا کرنے کے لئے نہ کہ باقی رکھنے کے لئے پس کھیتی اس سامان کے مشابہ ہوگئی جو مکان میں رکھا ہو۔ لہذا جس طرح مکان کی بیع میں سامان داخل نہیں ہوتا اس طرح زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی بھی داخل نہ ہوگی۔ ایک سوال اور اس کا جواب اگر یوں کیا جا کر مل مل کے ساتھ جدا ہونے کے واسطے متصل ہوتا ہے لیکن اس کے باوجود حمل ماں کی بیچ میں داخل ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ قاعدہ کے موافق داخل نہ ہونا چاہئیے ۔ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حمل کا جدا کرنا انسان کی قدرت میں نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ اللہ تعالیٰ کا کام ہے۔ اور کھیتی اور سامان کا جدا کرنا آدمی کی وسعت میں ہے۔ اس لئے حمل کو کھیتی اور مکان کے سامان پر قیاس کرنا درست نہ ہوگا ۔
And if there is a standing crop in the land, then the crop will not be included in the sale of the land. Yes, if at the time of the contract of sale the crop is mentioned along with the land, then the crop will be included. The proof is that the crop is attached to the land for separation, not for retention. Thus the crop resembles the goods that are placed in a house. Therefore, just as goods are not included in the sale of a house, similarly a crop is not included in the sale of land. A question and its answer: If it is said that the fetus is attached to the mother for separation too, yet the fetus is included in the sale of the mother, while according to the rule it should not be included. The answer to this is that separating the fetus is not within human power; rather, it is the work of Allah Almighty. And separating the crop and goods is within human capacity. Therefore, it is not correct to analogize the fetus to the crop and goods of a house.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents a second ruling: while trees are included, crops are not. The key distinction is the purpose of attachment: crops (kheti) are attached for separation (al-qat'), not for retention (al-baqa'), so they are analogous to movable goods (saman) in a house. Just as goods in a house do not pass with the sale of the house, crops do not pass with land. However, if explicitly mentioned, they become part of the sale. The passage then anticipates a counterargument: a fetus is attached to its mother for separation, yet it is included in the sale of the mother (i.e., a pregnant slave woman). The Hanafi response is that separating the fetus is beyond human capacity (it is a natural process from Allah), whereas crops and goods can be separated by humans. Therefore, the analogy (qiyas) fails. This rebuttal preserves the consistency of the rule by introducing the element of human capability.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): Fetus in the womb, used here to challenge the rule about attachment for separation.
اتصال للقطع (ittisal li al-qat'): Attachment for the purpose of cutting/removing, as opposed to attachment for permanence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because crops are not valuable.
B) Because crops are attached for removal, not permanence.
C) Because crops belong to the state.
D) Because the buyer does not need crops.

Key Takeaway
Standing crops are not automatically included in land sales because they are attached for removal, analogous to movable goods in a house.

Fruit on Trees Belongs to Seller Unless Buyer Stipulates
Ruling with hadith and rational proof that fruit stays with seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fruit ownership when a tree is sold, and identify the two proofs (hadith and analogy) that support it.

Core Idea
Fruit on a tree belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates otherwise.

This lesson from al-Hidayah clarifies a common point in sales: when you buy a fruit tree, does the fruit come with it? The Hanafi school says no – fruit is like a crop and remains the seller's property unless the buyer explicitly includes it. We'll examine the hadith proof and the rational analogy that underpin this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a fruit-bearing tree. The ruling is that the fruit belongs to the seller by default.
This is a ruling on ownership, not a taklifi hukm.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith: 'Man ishtara ardan fiha nakhil...' and the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar about slaves and pollinated trees.
Both hadith are ahad but sufficient for fiqh rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly states 'al-thamratu lil-ba'i' (fruit for seller) and the exception 'illa an yashtarita al-mubta'' indicates the buyer can acquire by stipulation.
The exception confirms the default rule.
4
Analyse the rational proof
The author draws an analogy (qiyas) to crops: fruit is attached naturally but meant for plucking, not permanence, so like crops it is not included.
This qiyas is based on the common illa of being intended for harvest.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad held that unripe fruit is included, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the default exclusion. The Shafi'i school also disagrees.
The tarjih is based on the zahir al-riwayah.

The sale of fruit is not included in the sale of the tree unless the buyer stipulates it. And whoever sold a palm tree or any fruit tree upon which there is fruit, then its fruit belongs to the seller, unless the buyer stipulates [otherwise]. Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever buys land in which there are palm trees, then the fruit belongs to the seller, unless the buyer stipulates.' And because the attachment of fruits to trees, even though it is natural, is for cutting, not for remaining, so they resemble crops. Explanation: The issue is that if someone sells a fruit-bearing tree, then its fruit belongs to the seller. Yes, if the buyer also purchases the fruits along with the tree, then the fruits are for the buyer. The proof is this hadith: 'Whoever buys land containing palm trees, then the fruit belongs to the seller, unless the buyer stipulates.' That is, if someone buys land with fruit trees, the fruit belongs to the seller, but if the buyer stipulates the fruit as well, then the fruit belongs to the buyer. A second hadith is from Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him). The words of the hadith are: 'Whoever sells a slave who has property, then that property belongs to the seller, unless the buyer stipulates. And whoever sells a palm tree that is pollinated, then the fruit belongs to the seller, unless the buyer stipulates.' This hadith also proves that the fruit of a tree belongs to the seller. The rational proof is that the attachment of fruits to trees, even if natural, is for plucking, not for remaining. So the fruit of the tree resembles crops. Thus, just as crops in land are not included in the sale of land, similarly fruits on trees are not included in the sale of trees.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage establishes a clear rule from the text of al-Hidayah: when a fruit-bearing tree is sold, the fruit on it remains the property of the seller unless the buyer explicitly stipulates its inclusion. Two proofs are provided: (1) a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) reported in various versions: 'Whoever buys land with palm trees, the fruit belongs to the seller unless the buyer stipulates.' (2) A rational proof (dalil 'aqli) based on analogy: the fruit's attachment is natural but intended for plucking (for cutting), not for permanence, thus it is like a crop (zar'). Just as a crop is not included in the sale of land without mention, fruit is not included in the sale of trees. The commentary expands by citing an additional hadith from Abdullah ibn Umar regarding slaves with property and pollinated palm trees, further supporting the ruling. The passage reaffirms that the default rule is seller ownership of fruit, and the buyer can acquire it only by explicit stipulation (shart).

Key Terms
ثمرہ (thamrah): Fruit on the tree at the time of sale, which remains seller's property unless buyer stipulates.
شرط (shart): A stipulation in the contract by which the buyer can include the fruit in the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer.
B) The seller.
C) The state.
D) It is shared equally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fruit on a tree belongs to the seller unless the buyer explicitly stipulates otherwise; this is supported by hadith and analogy to crops.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Seller must immediately remove fruit/crops from sold property
Ruling that seller must remove fruit/crops and deliver property free, with Shafi'i disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that the seller must immediately clear sold property of his fruit/crops, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on customary delivery.

Core Idea
Seller's property encumbers buyer's ownership — immediate removal is wajib, even if fruit is unripe.

When you buy a fruit tree or land with crops, the fruit and crops still belong to the seller. Does the seller have to cut them and leave the property free immediately, or can he wait until they ripen? The Hanafi school insists on immediate removal, while Imam al-Shafi'i permits a delay. This lesson explores the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the seller must immediately cut fruit and harvest crops, delivering the sold property free of his belongings. This is a direct consequence of the buyer's ownership being occupied (mashghul) by the seller's property.
The Hanafi school analogizes this to vacating a house from the seller's goods.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i, along with Malik and Ahmad, holds that the seller may leave fruit until it becomes good and crops until harvestable. Their basis is that the obligation is only customary delivery, which allows reasonable delay.
Custom (urf) plays a central role in the Shafi'i argument.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof (as partially presented) argues that customary practice does not require immediate cutting and that the seller's duty is fulfilled by delivering the property in a state that is customary, similar to how a lessee must vacate but may leave crops until harvest.
The passage is cut off, so we only have the beginning of their proof.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school counters by pointing to the lease analogy: even there, delivery is obligatory after term, and the land can be left on rent if needed. They argue that the same principle applies here – the seller's property is an encumbrance that must be removed immediately.
The Hanafi response also stresses that there is no distinction between valuable and non-valuable fruit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi gives the buyer immediate possession, seller bears cost of removal; Shafi'i allows the seller to delay until fruit/crops are ready, meaning the buyer takes possession later.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school is immediate removal.

کہ وہ صبیح کو فارغ کر کے مشتری کے حوالہ کر دے اس لئے پھل تو ڑ کر اور کھیتی کاٹ کر درخت اور زمین کا سپرد کرنا واجب ہے۔ اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسے کہ کسی نے مکان فروخت کیا اور مکان میں بائع کا سامان رکھا ہو تو بائع پر مکان خالی کر کے سپر د کرنا واجب ہے۔ امام شافعی کا مذہب حضرت امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ پھل درخت پر چھوڑ دیئے جائیں گے یہاں تک کہ ان کا کارآمد ہونا ظاہر ہو۔ اور کھیتی زمین میں چھوڑ دی جائے گی یہاں تک کہ وہ کاٹنے کے قابل ہو۔ حاصل یہ کہ درخت اور زمین کا فوری طور پر سپرد کرنا واجب نہیں ہے بلکہ پچھلوں کو کارآمد ہونے اور کھیتی کو کاٹنے کے قابل ہونے تک چھوڑ دیا جائے گا۔ یہی قول امام مالک اور امام احمد کا ہے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل ... ان حضرات کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع پر میچ کا سپرد کرنا بلاشبہ واجب ہے لیکن اس طرح سپرد کرنا واجب ہے جو دلیل
And it will be said to the seller: cut them (the fruit) and hand over the sold item. Similarly, if there is crop in it, because the ownership of the buyer is occupied by the ownership of the seller, so it is incumbent upon him to vacate it and hand it over, just as if there were goods in it. Imam al-Shafi'i said: It should be left until the fruit becomes good and the crop is ready to harvest, because the obligation is only customary delivery, and customarily one does not cut like that. And it becomes like when the period of lease ends and there is crop in the land. We say: There too delivery is obligatory, so much so that the land is left on rent. And handing over the compensation (wage) is like handing over the compensated property (land). And there is no difference whether the fruit is in a state where it has value or not. In the correct opinion, in both cases it belongs to the seller, because its sale is permissible in the more authentic of the two narrations, as we will explain. Thus this fruit is not included in the sale of the tree without mention. Explanation: It has already passed in the first issue that if someone sold a fruit-bearing tree, then the fruit belongs to the seller. So when the fruit belongs to the seller and the tree belongs to the buyer, then the seller will be told to cut the fruit and hand over the sold item to the buyer. Similarly, if land is sold and there is crop in the land, then too the seller will be told to harvest his crop and hand over the land to the buyer. The proof is that in both mentioned situations, the buyer's property (the tree and land) is occupied by the seller's property (the fruit and crop). Yet it is obligatory upon the seller to free the sold item and deliver it to the buyer. Therefore, it is obligatory to cut the fruit and harvest the crop and hand over the tree and land. And this is like the case where someone sold a house and there was the seller's goods in the house: the seller is obligated to vacate the house and hand it over. The school of Imam al-Shafi'i: Hadrat Imam al-Shafi'i said that the fruit will be left on the tree until its beneficial condition appears, and the crop will be left in the land until it is ready to harvest. The conclusion is that it is not obligatory to immediately hand over the tree and land; rather, they will be left until the fruit is beneficial and the crop is harvestable. This is also the view of Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof... Their proof is that delivering the sold item is undoubtedly obligatory, but it must be delivered in the way that is...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage addresses the practical consequence of the previous rulings: since fruit and crops belong to the seller, and the tree/land belong to the buyer, the seller is obligated to remove his property (cut fruit, harvest crops) and deliver the sold item free to the buyer. This is like vacating a house from goods. The Hanafi position is that this obligation is immediate. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the seller can leave the fruit/crops until they ripen or mature for harvest, arguing that customary delivery allows delay and that the buyer's ownership is not fully obstructed. The Hanafi response (partially given) points out that in the case of an expiring lease, the tenant must also vacate, and the land can be rented out if needed. The Hanafi school insists that the seller's property (fruit/crop) is a barrier (shughl) to the buyer's full ownership, so immediate removal is wajib. The passage is cut off before completing the Shafi'i proof and Hanafi rebuttal. The relied-upon position is that the seller must cut immediately, regardless of whether the fruit has value or not, because the sale of such fruit is permissible per the stronger narration.

Key Terms
تفریغ (tafriqh): The act of vacating or clearing the sold item from the seller's property (fruit/crop) before delivery.
مشغول (mashghul): The state of the buyer's property being occupied or encumbered by the seller's property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must the seller do with fruit belonging to him that is on the tree the buyer just purchased?
A) Leave the fruit until it ripens.
B) Cut the fruit immediately and deliver the tree.
C) Wait until the buyer agrees on a time.
D) Share the fruit with the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The seller must immediately remove his property from the sold item to free the buyer's ownership; this is a binding obligation in the Hanafi school, contrary to the Shafi'i allowance for delay.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Seller Must Cut Fruit and Crops Before Delivery
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on fruit/crops in land/tree sales, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the analogy to lease.

Core Idea
The seller's ownership of fruit/crops encumbers the buyer's property; delivery requires vacating that encumbrance.

When you buy a tree with fruit or land with crops, who owns the fruit? The Hanafi school says the seller must cut and deliver the property free of his property. But Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that custom allows leaving fruit until ripe. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the Hanafi proof by analogy to vacating goods from a house.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
Identify that the seller must cut fruit/harvest crop and deliver the tree/land free. The basis is that the buyer's ownership is encumbered (mashghūl) by the seller's property; delivery requires vacating that encumbrance.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the fruit has value.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that fruit/crops should be left until they are usable (ripe/harvestable). His proof: customary delivery does not require immediate cutting, and analogy to lease where a tenant's crop remains after the lease ends.
Malik and Ahmad agree with al-Shafi'i.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school rebuts the lease analogy: even in lease, immediate delivery is obligatory; if the crop remains, additional rent is due. Thus the analogy fails, and the obligation to vacate remains.
The seller must vacate, not leave his property in the buyer's possession.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Hanafi seller must cut fruit before handing over the tree; a Shafi'i seller may leave it until ripe, benefiting the buyer without immediate cutting.
This affects timing of delivery and potential disputes.
5
Confirm the tarjih and mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The soundest narration holds that selling fruit separately is valid, so it does not enter the sale of trees.
Ashraf ul Hadaya affirms this tarjih.

It will be said to the seller: cut it and deliver the sold item. Likewise if there is crop in it, because the buyer's ownership is occupied by the seller's ownership, so it is incumbent on him to vacate and deliver, as if there was goods in it. Al-Shafi'i said: it is left until the fruit's ripeness appears and the crop is harvested, because the obligation is only customary delivery, and customarily it is not cut like that. And it becomes like when the lease period ends and there is crop in the land. We said: there too delivery is obligatory, so it is left with rent, and delivery of the consideration is like delivery of the subject matter. And there is no difference between whether the fruit has value or not, according to the correct view. In both cases it belongs to the seller, because its sale is valid in the soundest of the two narrations, as we will explain, so it is not included in the sale of trees without mention.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. The main ruling: when fruit-bearing trees or land with crops are sold, the fruit/crop belongs to the seller. Therefore the seller is ordered to cut the fruit and harvest the crop before delivering the tree or land to the buyer. The reason is that the buyer's ownership (of the tree/land) is encumbered by the seller's ownership (of the fruit/crop). The seller must vacate it, analogous to a house where the seller has goods stored. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that customary delivery does not require immediate cutting; rather the fruit should be left until it ripens and the crop until it is harvestable, based on custom and analogy to a lease where a tenant's crop remains after the lease ends. The Hanafi response: even in lease, immediate delivery is obligatory, and if the crop remains, additional rent is due. Thus the analogy fails. The text then clarifies that it makes no difference whether the fruit has market value or not; in both cases it belongs to the seller because selling fruit separately is valid according to the soundest narration. Therefore, fruit is not included in the sale of trees unless explicitly mentioned.

Key Terms
مشغول (mashghūl): Occupied or encumbered; here meaning the buyer's property is entangled with the seller's property (fruit/crop) which occupies the same physical space.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery of the sold item from seller to buyer, which must be complete and free of encumbrances.
ثمر (thamar): Fruit; the produce of a tree that may be sold separately or attached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, when trees with fruit are sold, what must the seller do?
A) Leave the fruit until it ripens
B) Cut the fruit and deliver the trees to the buyer
C) Give the fruit to the buyer
D) Wait for mutual agreement

Key Takeaway
The seller must remove his fruit/crops before delivering sold land/trees, regardless of value, because complete delivery requires vacating any encumbrance.

Seeds and Crops in Land Sale: Hanafi Inclusion Ruling
Ruling on whether sown seeds or sprouted crops are included in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether seeds and crops are included in a land sale, distinguishing between stages of growth, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on sprouted seeds with no value.

Core Idea
Sprouted seeds with no value are disputed: are they part of the land or not?

When land is sold, what happens to seeds already sown by the seller? The answer depends on whether seeds have sprouted, whether they have value, and how the contract is worded. This passage from the Hanafi legal tradition clarifies these distinctions, noting an internal disagreement when the crop is immature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage discusses three situations: (1) seeds not sprouted, (2) seeds sprouted but with no value, (3) harvested fruit/crop. For each, the ruling on inclusion differs.
2
Understand the legal basis
Unsprouted seeds are like deposited goods (wadī‘ah), so they remain the seller's property. Sprouted seeds with no value involve an intra-Hanafi disagreement based on whether selling immature crops is valid.
The root ikhtilaf: if immature crop cannot be sold separately, it is considered part of the land; if it can, it is separate.
3
Analyze contract phrasing
Crops are not included by mentioning 'rights and appurtenances'. However, if the seller says 'every little and much that he has in it' without limiting to rights, crops are included. Adding 'from its rights' or 'from its appurtenances' excludes them.
The phrase 'in it and from its rights' is restrictive; 'in it' alone is inclusive.
4
Distinguish harvested vs. growing
Plucked fruit and harvested crops are like goods and are never included unless explicitly named. This applies regardless of value or growth stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما اذا بيعت الارض وقد بذرفيها صاحبها ولم ينبت بعد لم يدخل فيه لانه مودع فيها كا لمتاع ولو نبت ولم تصر له قيمة فقد قيل لايدخل فيه وقد قيل يدخل فيه وكان هذا بناء على الاختلاف في جواز بیعه قبل ان بناله المشافرو المناجل ولايد خل الزرع والثمر بذكر الحقوق والمرافق لانهما ليسا منهما ولو قال بكل قليل وكثير هو له فيها ومنها من حقوقها اوقال من مرافقها لم يدخلافيه لما قلنا وان لم يقل من حقوقها اومن مرافقها دخلافيه اما الثمر المجذوذ والزرع المحصود لايدخل الابالتصريح به لانه بمنزلة المتاع
ترجمہ اور اگر زمین فروخت کی گئی اور حال یہ کہ مالک نے اس میں بیج ڈال دیا ہے اور وہ ابھی تک جمانہیں ہے تو بیج زمین کی بیچ میں داخل نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ پیج سامان کے مانند اس میں رکھا ہوا ہے۔ اور اگر بیج اگ آیا اور ابھی اس کی کوئی قیمت نہیں ہوئی ہے۔ تو کہا گیا کہ و کھیتی ، زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگی اور کہا گیا کہ داخل ہو جائے گی ۔ گویا یہ اختلاف اس بناء پر ہے کہ درانتیوں اور ہونٹوں سے کاٹنے کے
And if the land is sold and its owner has sown seeds in it and they have not sprouted yet, they are not included in it because they are deposited in it like goods. And if they sprouted but have not acquired value, it is said they are not included, and it is said they are included. This is based on the disagreement regarding the validity of selling it before it reaches the stage of sickles and scythes. And the crop and fruit are not included by mentioning rights and appurtenances, because they are not from them. And if he says 'with every little and much that he has in it and from its rights', or says 'from its appurtenances', they are not included for what we said. And if he does not say 'from its rights' or 'from its appurtenances', they are included. As for the plucked fruit and harvested crop, they are not included except by explicit mention, because they are like goods.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling about whether seeds sown in land are included when the land is sold. The ruling distinguishes based on the stage of growth. (1) If seeds have been sown but have not yet sprouted, they are not included in the sale of the land because they are like deposited goods (وديعة) in the land. The buyer does not acquire them. (2) If the seeds have sprouted but have not yet acquired any market value, there are two opinions within the Hanafi school: one says they are not included, another says they are included. This difference stems from a deeper disagreement about whether it is permissible to sell such immature crops separately (before they are ready for harvesting with sickles and scythes). If selling them separately is not valid, they might be considered part of the land; if valid, they are separate. (3) The passage then states a general principle: mentioning 'rights' (حقوق) or 'appurtenances' (مرافق) does not include crop or fruit. But if the seller says 'every little and much that he has in it and from its rights', it still does not include crop/fruit. However, if he does not limit with 'from its rights' or 'from its appurtenances', then such a broad phrase might include them. Finally, already harvested fruit or cut crop are not included unless explicitly mentioned, as they are like goods. This reflects the Hanafi methodology of treating growing crops as attached to the land or not based on their legal status and custom.

Key Terms
مودع (mūda‘): Deposited; here meaning seeds are placed in the land as a deposit (wadī‘ah) and thus belong to the depositor (seller), not included in sale.
الحقوق والمرافق (al-ḥuqūq wa al-marāfiq): Rights and appurtenances; legal entitlements and physical attachments that come with the property but do not include growing crops.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If land is sold and the seller has sown seeds that have not yet sprouted, are the seeds included in the sale?
A) Yes, because they are part of the land
B) No, because they are like deposited goods
C) Yes, if the buyer agrees
D) No, because they belong to the buyer automatically

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, unsprouted seeds are never automatically included in land sale; they are deposits. Sprouted seeds with no value are disputed, but the dominant view is they also are not included.

Valueless Fruits in Sale of Trees: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inclusion of valueless fruits in tree sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi position on inclusion of valueless fruits in a tree sale and evaluate the opposing view within the school.

Core Idea
Even valueless fruits remain the seller's property unless explicitly included in the sale contract.

Imagine buying a fruit orchard. Are the tiny, unripe fruits on the trees yours? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on this point. The relied-upon position is that such fruits, no matter how small or worthless, belong to the seller unless the contract explicitly includes them. Let's explore the reasoning and why the other view is considered less sound.

Guided Path
1
State the Two Positions
Identify the correct (sahih qawl) position and the opposing view within the Hanafi school. The correct position: fruits are not included in the sale of trees regardless of value. The opposing view: valueless fruits are included because they lack independent market value.
2
Examine the Proof for Each Position
For the correct position: even tiny fruits can be sold separately (e.g., as animal fodder), so they are not part of the tree. For the opposing view: valueless fruits have no independent commercial value, so they become part of the tree like its branches.
Note that both proofs focus on the principle of separate salability versus permanent attachment.
3
Apply the Hanafi Rule on Inclusion
The correct position relies on the rule: things that can be sold separately are not included in the sale of another item unless they are permanently attached (ittisal 'ala sabil al-qarar). Fruits are temporarily attached, so the rule applies.
Permanent attachment would include buildings, roots, etc.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: under the correct position, the seller keeps the fruits even after sale; under the opposing view, the buyer gets them. This affects harvest rights and pricing.
The difference is only for valueless fruits; if fruits have value, both views agree they belong to seller.
5
Determine the Tarjih
The author (Sahib al-Hidayah) states that the 'sahih qawl' (correct saying) is that fruits are not included. This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.
The opposing view is considered weak (marjuh) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فرق ما بين ما اذ.... الخ سے صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ پھل اس حال میں ہوں کہ ان کی کوئی قیمت ہے یا ان کی کوئی قیمت نہ ہو بایں طور کہ بالکل چھوٹے چھوٹے ہیں تو صحیح قول کے مطابق دونوں صورتوں میں پھل بائع ہی کے ہوں گے ۔ بعض حضرات کا خیال ہے کہ پھل اگر اس قدر چھوٹے ہوں کہ ان کی بازار میں کوئی مالیت اور قیمت نہ ہو تو وہ درختوں کی بیج میں داخل ہوں گے اور مشتری کے لئے ہوں گے ۔ قول صحیح کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ پھل خواہ ان کی کوئی قیمت ہو یا نہ ہو دونوں صورتوں میں ان کا تنہا فروخت کرنا جائز ہے۔ اور جن چیزوں کا تنہا بیچنا جائز ہوتا ہے وہ دوسری چیز کی بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوتیں بشرطیکہ ان کا اتصال علی سبیل القرار نہ ہو ۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ پچھل درختوں کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوں گے الا یہ کہ پھلوں کا ذکر کر دیا ہو۔
And there is no difference between what has no value and what has value... etc. From Sahib al-Hidayah: says that fruits whether they have value or not, if they are very small, according to the correct saying in both cases the fruits belong to the seller. Some scholars opine that if the fruits are so small that they have no market value, then they are included in the sale of the trees and belong to the buyer. The reason for the correct saying is that fruits, whether they have value or not, can be sold separately in both cases. And things that can be sold separately are not included in the sale of another thing, provided they are not permanently attached. So it is established that they will not be included in the sale of the trees unless the fruits are mentioned.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding fruits on trees at the time of sale. The relied-upon position (sahih qawl) is that fruits are not included in the sale of trees, regardless of whether they have market value or are extremely small and worthless. The opposing view holds that valueless fruits are included because they lack independent commercial value. The justification for the correct position is that even tiny fruits can be sold separately (e.g., for animal fodder), and the general principle is that anything that can be sold independently is not included in the sale of another item unless explicitly mentioned, as long as it is not permanently attached (ittisal 'ala sabil al-qarar). Since fruits are temporarily attached, they remain the seller's property. The key lesson is that the default rule is non-inclusion except by explicit mention.

Key Terms
قول صحیح (qawl sahih): The correct and relied-upon position in the Hanafi school
اتصال علی سبیل القرار (ittisal 'ala sabil al-qarar): Permanent attachment intended to last

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They are not included in the sale because even valueless fruits can be sold separately.
C) They are included only if the fruits are ripe.
D) They are owned jointly by buyer and seller.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: items that can be sold separately are not included in the sale of another unless permanently attached, regardless of their market value.

The Status of Unsprouted Seed and Sprouted Seed Without Value
Matn from Al-Hidayah on seed in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for seed in land sale (unsprouted and sprouted with no value) and explain the basis of the two narrations.

Core Idea
Unsprouted seed is like a deposit; sprouted seed with no value has two narrations.

In Hanafi sales law, the inclusion of seed in a land sale depends on its stage. If the seed has not yet sprouted, it is considered a deposited item (muda') and remains with the seller. If it has sprouted but has no marketable value, the sources present two narrations, with the stronger view being that it is also not included. This lesson examines these two scenarios and the underlying rational analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between seed that has not yet sprouted and seed that has sprouted but has no value. Both are cases where the seed is physically present but not yet a valuable crop.
2
Understand the ruling for unsprouted seed
The text states: 'the seed is not included because it is deposited in it like baggage.' This is a qiyas (analogy) to a deposited item (wadi'ah) that remains the property of the depositor.
The seed is not part of the land; it is a separate property.
3
Recognize the two narrations for sprouted seed without value
The passage says: 'and if it sprouted but has no value, it has been said that it is not included and it has been said that it is included.' This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The disagreement stems from whether such greenery has enough value to be sold independently.
4
Trace the underlying principle
The ability to sell the crop independently (before it is harvestable) is the basis. If it cannot be sold separately, it is considered part of the land; if it can, it is not included.
The benchmark is whether the crop 'can be reached by sickles and lips' (mashafir wa manajil), indicating harvestability.
5
Apply the preferred view
The preferred (mufta bihi) position, consistent with the analogy to a deposit, is that the sprouted seed with no value is also not included. This aligns with the stronger principle that the seed is not part of the land.
The text presents both narrations without explicit tarjih, but the first narration (not included) is more consistent with the unsprouted case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما اذا بيعت الارض وقد بذرفيها صاحبها ولم ينبت بعد لم يدخل فيه لانه مودع فيها كا لمتاع ولو نبت ولم تصر له قيمة فقد قيل لايدخل فيه وقد قيل يدخل فيه وكان هذا بناء على الاختلاف في جواز بیعه قبل ان بناله المشافرو المناجل ولايد خل الزرع والثمر بذكر الحقوق والمرافق لانهما ليسا منهما ولو قال بكل قليل وكثير هو له فيها ومنها من حقوقها اوقال من مرافقها لم يدخلافيه لما قلنا وان لم يقل من حقوقها اومن مرافقها دخلافيه اما الثمر المجذوذ والزرع المحصود لايدخل الابالتصريح به لانه بمنزلة المتاع
And if the land is sold while its owner has sown seed in it and it has not yet sprouted, the seed is not included because it is deposited in it like baggage. And if it sprouted but has no value, it has been said that it is not included and it has been said that it is included, and this is based on the disagreement about the permissibility of selling it before it can be reached by sickles and lips. And the crop and fruit are not included by mentioning rights and facilities because they are not from them. And if he said 'with every small and large thing that belongs to him in it and from its rights' or said 'from its facilities', they are not included for the reason we said. And if he did not say 'from its rights' or 'from its facilities', they are included. As for the picked fruit and harvested crop, they are not included except by explicit mention because they are like baggage.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a foundational matn from Al-Hidayah covering several key rules for the sale of land with crops and trees with fruit. 1) Seed not yet sprouted is not included in the sale of land because it is considered a deposited item (muda') like luggage in a house. 2) Sprouted seed with no value (tiny shoots) has two narrations: some say included, some not. This difference stems from whether such greenery can be sold independently (some allow it, others not). If it can be sold separately, it stays with seller; if not, it becomes part of the land. 3) If the seller mentions 'rights' (huquq) and 'facilities' (marafiq), the crop and fruit are still not included because they are not part of those ancillary rights. However, if he says 'everything small and large that belongs to him in it and from its rights' without the restrictive phrase 'from its rights', then the general wording includes crops and fruit. 4) Already harvested fruit and cut crops are never included unless explicitly stated, as they are movable property like baggage. The key principle is that unharvested crops and fruit are separate property unless specifically mentioned.

Key Terms
مودع (muda'): Deposited item, considered separate from the land like a trust
مشافر ومناجل (mashafir wa manajil): Lips (of livestock) and sickles (of farmers) – indicators that the crop is harvestable

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
