A) It is included in the sale of the land.
B) It is not included, as it is still like a deposit.
C) It is included only if the seller intended it.
D) It is not included because it has value.

Key Takeaway
Unsprouted seed and sprouted seed with no value are not included in the sale of land; they remain the seller's property.

The Effect of Mentioning Rights and Facilities on Including Crops and Fruit
Matn on wording effect for crops in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between wording that includes crops/fruit in a land sale and wording that excludes them, based on the Hanafi rules for general and restricted expressions.

Core Idea
Mentioning 'rights and facilities' does not include crops; general wording without restriction does.

In land or tree sales, the words used matter. If the seller says 'with all rights and facilities', that does not automatically include standing crops or fruit. But if he uses a general expression like 'with everything small and large' without restricting it to rights, then the crops and fruit are included. This lesson dissects the language used in the contract.

Guided Path
1
Define 'rights and facilities' (huquq wa marafiq)
These are ancillary entitlements like pathways, water rights, etc. They do not include the produce of the land or trees.
The text explicitly says: 'crop and fruit are not included by mentioning rights and facilities because they are not from them.'
2
Understand the base wording without restriction
If the seller says: 'with every small and large thing that belongs to him in it and from its rights' (wa lam yaqul min huquqiha), the phrase 'from its rights' acts as a restriction. Without that restriction, the general expression is inclusive.
The text: 'and if he did not say from its rights or from its facilities, they are included.'
3
Contrast with the restrictive phrase
If the seller adds 'from its rights' or 'from its facilities', the expression is limited to rights and facilities only, so crops and fruit are excluded.
The text: 'if he said with every small and large thing... from its rights... they are not included.'
4
Apply the rule to standing crops and unharvested fruit
Standing crops and unharvested fruit are not part of huquq wa marafiq. Therefore, they are only included if the contract language is general and unrestricted.
The key is the presence or absence of the phrase 'min huquqiha' or 'min marafiqiha'.
5
Identify the underlying principle
Hanafi law treats crops and fruit as separate property. General words of sale include them unless an exception is made. The mention of rights and facilities is a specific exception that excludes produce.
This is a matter of contractual interpretation based on urf (custom) and linguistic precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Inclusion of Harvested Produce in Land Sale
Matn on harvested produce in land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for harvested fruit and cut crops in a land sale and explain why they are treated like movable property.

Core Idea
Harvested fruit and cut crops are movable property; they are not included in land sale unless explicitly mentioned.

Once fruit is picked or crops are harvested, they become separate movable items, analogous to baggage. Therefore, when selling land or trees, such harvested produce is not automatically transferred to the buyer. Only explicit mention in the contract includes them. This lesson focuses on this final part of the Hidayah passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items in question
The text specifically mentions 'picked fruit' (al-thamar al-majdudh) and 'harvested crop' (al-zar' al-mahsud). These are no longer attached to the land or trees.
2
Understand their legal status
Once detached, they are considered movable property (manqulat), like baggage (mata'). They are not part of the real estate.
The text says: 'they are like baggage.'
3
State the ruling
Harvested produce is not included in the sale of land or trees unless explicitly stated in the contract.
The text: 'they are not included except by explicit mention.'
4
Contrast with standing crops
Unlike standing (unharvested) crops, which may be included under certain wording, harvested produce is always separate and requires explicit sale.
This distinction prevents confusion.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a farmer sells his orchard and there is a pile of harvested apples, the buyer does not own those apples unless the contract says so.
The buyer may need to purchase them separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a foundational matn from Al-Hidayah covering several key rules for the sale of land with crops and trees with fruit. 1) Seed not yet sprouted is not included in the sale of land because it is considered a deposited item (muda') like luggage in a house. 2) Sprouted seed with no value (tiny shoots) has two narrations: some say included, some not. This difference stems from whether such greenery can be sold independently (some allow it, others not). If it can be sold separately, it stays with seller; if not, it becomes part of the land. 3) If the seller mentions 'rights' (huquq) and 'facilities' (marafiq), the crop and fruit are still not included because they are not part of those ancillary rights. However, if he says 'everything small and large that belongs to him in it and from its rights' without the restrictive phrase 'from its rights', then the general wording includes crops and fruit. 4) Already harvested fruit and cut crops are never included unless explicitly stated, as they are movable property like baggage. The key principle is that unharvested crops and fruit are separate property unless specifically mentioned.

Key Terms
ماع (mata'): Baggage or movable property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is harvested fruit included in a land sale according to this passage?
A) Always included because it comes from the land.
B) Included only if the contract explicitly mentions it.
C) Included if the fruit is still in the orchard.
D) Never included.

Key Takeaway
Harvested fruit and cut crops are movable property and require explicit mention in the sale contract to be transferred.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Inclusion of Crops and Fruits: The Four Wordings
Explains inclusion of crops/fruits based on four wording types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four types of wording used in the sale of land with crops or trees with fruits and determine whether crops/fruits are included according to Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Crops and fruits are only included if the seller uses general wording without mentioning rights or appurtenances.

In Hanafi sales law, the inclusion of accessory items like standing crops or hanging fruits depends on the exact phrasing used by the seller. This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah breaks down four categories of wording and their legal effect. By learning these categories, you will be able to predict the outcome of a sale contract involving land with crops or trees with fruits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of land with standing crops or tree with attached fruits. The ruling is that inclusion of crops/fruits depends on the wording used. Determine the default rule: without any mention, they are not included.
2
Catalog the four wordings
List the four types of phrases a seller might use: (1) bare sale, (2) with 'rights and appurtenances', (3) 'every small and great from its rights/appurtenances', (4) 'every small and great in it'. Decide for each whether crops/fruits are included.
Only the fourth wording includes attached crops/fruits.
3
Understand the legal basis
Learn why rights (huquq) and appurtenances (marafiq) do not include crops/fruits. Huquq are essential incorporeal rights like water access; marafiq are physical adjuncts like outbuildings. Crops and fruits are separate assets, not accessories.
4
Apply the rule to attached vs. detached
Note that even with the fourth wording, only standing crops and hanging fruits are included. If they are plucked or cut, they are like stored goods and are not included unless explicitly mentioned.
The analogy (qiyas) is to goods stored in a house: not included in the sale of the house.

Ashrāf al-Hidāyah commentary on Hidāyah volume eight, book of sales. There is disagreement about the validity of sale before eligibility. And with mention of rights and appurtenances, crops and fruits are not included, because these two are not from rights and appurtenances. And if the seller said 'with every small and great that is in this sale item or from it from its rights or from its appurtenances', still crops and fruits are not included, because of the proof we mentioned. And if he did not say 'from its rights or from its appurtenances', then crops and fruits become included in the sale. As for plucked fruits and cut crops, they are not included except with explicit mention, because they are like movable goods. Explanation: The scenario is that a person sold his land while he had planted seeds in it and the seeds have not yet germinated. Then the seeds are not included in the sale of the land; rather the seeds belong to the seller and the land belongs to the buyer. The proof is that the seeds are stored in the land, and a stored item is not included in the sale, just as goods stored in a house are not included in the sale of the house. In some fatwa books it is stated that if the seeds have rotted, then they become subsidiary to the land and are included in the sale, belonging to the buyer, because the sale of rotten grain alone is not valid, so it becomes subsidiary to the land. Yes, if it has not rotted, then the seeds are not included in the sale and belong to the seller. And if the seeds sprouted and tiny shoots emerged, but they have no market value, some scholars opine that the greenery is not included in the sale of the land, while others opine that it is included. According to the first view, the greenery belongs to the seller, and according to the second, to the buyer. The author of Hidāyah says this difference is based on another difference: whether such greenery that cannot be cut with sickles nor can animals grasp it with their lips (i.e., the shoots have just emerged from the ground) can be sold alone or not. Some said it is valid, some said invalid. Those who said its sole sale is valid hold that the greenery is not subsidiary to the land and is not included, so it belongs to the seller. Those who said its sole sale is invalid hold that it is subsidiary and included, so both go to the buyer. 'ولا يدخل الزرع' etc. There is mention of different phrases for land with standing crops and tree with fruits. In some cases crops and fruits are included, in some not. The detail: Four types of wording may be used: 1) The owner said 'bactu al-ard' or 'bactu al-shajara' — I sold the land or the tree, and nothing more. The ruling has passed earlier that crops are not included in sale of land, nor fruits in sale of tree. 2) The owner said 'bactu bi-huquqiha wa marafiqiha' — I sold the land or tree with its rights and appurtenances. 3) He said 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fiha wa minha min huquqiha aw min marafiqiha' — I sold with every small and great that is in it or from it from its rights or appurtenances. 4) 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fiha' — I sold with every small and great that is in it, without mentioning rights or appurtenances. In the second and third cases, crops and fruits are not included because the word 'rights' or 'appurtenances' is mentioned, and crops and fruits are neither rights nor appurtenances. Rights are those accessories necessary for the sold item and intended because of it, like irrigation water and path. Appurtenances are those accessories that are attached and specific to the sold item, like a washroom, kitchen, and drainage path of a house. Since crops are not rights or appurtenances of land, and fruits are not of trees, mentioning them does not include crops or fruits; they remain the seller's. In the fourth case, when rights and appurtenances are not mentioned, then both crops and fruits are included because of the general word 'every small and great that is in it', but this is when the crops are standing and fruits are attached. If fruits are plucked and crops are cut, even with 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin', they are not included because they are like stored goods, and stored goods are not included as subsidiary. However, if explicitly mentioned, then cut crops and plucked fruits become included and belong to the buyer.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah provides a detailed ruling on when standing crops (زرع) and attached fruits (ثمر) are included in the sale of land and trees. The author begins by stating a general principle: if the seller mentions 'rights' (حقوق) or 'appurtenances' (مرافق), crops and fruits are not included because they do not fall under those categories. If the seller does not mention them, then crops and fruits are included, but only if they are still attached (standing or hanging). Plucked fruits and cut crops are treated like movable goods and require explicit mention to be included. The commentary then elaborates on specific scenarios involving seeds that have not yet sprouted. The ruling here is clear: seeds that are simply stored in the ground (not yet sprouted) are like goods stored in a house and are not included in the sale of the land. However, if the seeds have rotted, they become subsidiary to the land and are included because they are no longer independently saleable. A dispute is noted among later scholars regarding seeds that have sprouted but are so tiny that they have no market value and cannot be cut or grazed. This dispute is traced back to a disagreement on whether such tiny sprouts can be sold independently. If they can be sold independently, they are not subsidiary and belong to the seller; if not, they become subsidiary and belong to the buyer. Finally, the passage systematically categorizes four types of wording in the sale of land with crops or trees with fruits, summarizing the rulings for each case. The key legal principle is the distinction between 'rights' (indispensable accessories like water rights) and 'appurtenances' (non-essential but attached features like outbuildings), and that crops and fruits fall into neither category unless explicitly included through general wording like 'بكل قليل وكثير' (with everything small and great).

Key Terms
حقوق (huqūq): Rights or accessories that are necessary for the sold item and intended because of it, e.g., irrigation water and right of way.
مرافق (marāfiq): Appurtenances that are attached to the property and exclusive to it, e.g., a washroom, kitchen, or drainage path. These are physical adjuncts.
تابع (tābiʿ): That which follows the principal (متبوع) in legal rulings; a subsidiary that is automatically included in the sale of the principal under certain conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller says 'بعت الأرض بكل قلیل و کثیر فیها من حقوقها' (I sold the land with every small and great in it from its rights), are standing crops included according to Hanafi law?
A) Yes, because the phrase 'every small and great' is general.
B) No, because the phrase restricts to rights only.
C) Yes, if the crops are ripe.
D) No, because crops are never included in land sales.

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of crops and fruits in a sale of land or trees depends on the wording: if the seller mentions 'rights' or 'appurtenances', they are excluded; if general wording is used without such mention, they are included only if still attached.

Inclusion of Seeds and Sprouts: The Status of Unplanted and Immature Crops
Explains inclusion of unplanted seeds and tiny sprouts in sale of land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules to determine whether seeds and tiny sprouts are included in the sale of land, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on sprouts.

Core Idea
Seeds stored in the ground are like goods in a house and not included; but if they rot or sprout, their status depends on independent saleability.

What happens to seeds already planted in the land when it is sold? And what about tiny sprouts that have barely emerged? This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah provides detailed rulings on these scenarios, including an intra-Hanafi dispute on sprouts that have no market value. By mastering these distinctions, you will understand how the concept of subsidiary (tābiʿ) applies to agricultural sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers three scenarios: (1) seeds that have not germinated, (2) seeds that have rotted, (3) tiny sprouts with no market value. Note the ruling for each.
2
Understand the proof for ungerminated seeds
The ruling that ungerminated seeds are not included is based on qiyas: seeds stored in the ground are analogous to goods stored in a house. Both are movable items not part of the real property.
This is a clear qiyas jaliy.
3
Identify the exception for rotted seeds
If seeds have rotted, they become subsidiary because they can no longer be sold independently. The criterion is independent saleability (bayʿ mufrad).
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on sprouts
For tiny sprouts with no market value, there are two opinions. The dispute is resolved by looking at whether the sprouts can be sold alone. If yes, they are independent and not included; if no, they are subsidiary and included.
The author refrains from tarjih.
5
Apply the concept of tābiʿ
Note that the subsidiary (tābiʿ) principle is used: an item that cannot be sold independently becomes subsidiary to the principal (land) and is included in the sale without explicit mention.

غرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم كتاب البيوع اہل ہونے سے پہلے اس کی بیع جائز ہونے میں اختلاف ہے اور حقوق اور مرافق ذکر کرنے کے ساتھ کھیتی اور پھل داخل نہ ہوں گے لیونکہ یہ دونوں حقوق و مرافق سے نہیں ہیں۔ اور اگر بائع نے کہا ہر قلیل و کثیر کے ساتھ جو اس مبیع میں یا مبیع سے اس کے حقوق یا مرافق میں سے ہو تو بھی کھیتی و پھل داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی۔ اور اگر اس حقوق سے یا اس کے مرافق سے نہیں کہا تو کھیتی اور پھل بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے۔ رہے توڑے ہوئے پھل اور کاٹی ہوئی کھیتی تو وہ داخل نہیں ہوگی مگر اسکی صراحت کرنے کے ساتھ ۔ کیونکہ یہ بمنزلہ سامان کے ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص نے اپنی زمین اس حال میں فروخت کی کہ وہ اس زمین میں بیج ڈال چکا ہے اور وہ بیج ابھی تک گیا نہیں ہے تو یہ بیج زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا بلکہ بیج بائع کا ہوگا اور زمین مشتری کی ہوگی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیج زمین میں رکھا ہوا ہے ۔ اور رکھی ہوئی چیز بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوتی ، جیسے وہ سامان جو مکان میں رکھا ہوا ہے مکان کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا۔ بعض فتاوی کی کتابوں میں ہے کہ بیج اگر گل سڑ گیا تو وہ زمین کے تابع ہو کر زمین کی بیع میں داخل ہو جائے گا۔ اور مشتری کے لئے ہوگا۔ کیونکہ گلے سڑے اناج کی بیع تنہا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے وہ زمین کے تابع ہو گا۔ ہاں اگر گلا سڑا نہیں تو وہ بیج زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا۔ اور وہ بائع کے لئے ہوگا۔ اور اگر وہ بیج اگ آیا اور ذرہ ذراسی کو پہلے نکل آئیں لیکن اس حالت میں ہے کہ بازار میں اس کی کوئی قیمت نہیں ہے تو بعض حضرات کا خیال ہے کہ یہ سبزہ زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا اور بعض کی رائے ہے کہ داخل ہو جائے گا۔ پہلے قول کی بناء پر یہ سبزہ بائع کے لئے ہوگا اور دوسرے قول کی بناء پر مشتری کے لئے ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ اختلاف ایک دوسرے اختلاف پر مبنی ہے ۔ وہ یہ ہے کہ ایسا سبزہ جس کو نہ تو درانتیوں سے کاٹا جا سکتا ہے اور نہ جانور اپنے ہونٹوں سے اس کو پکڑ کر چھ سکتے ہیں۔ یعنی کو پہلے ابھی زمین سے باہر نکلی ہیں تو ایسے سبزے کا تنہا بیچنا جائز ہے یا جائز نہیں ہے۔ بعض حضرات نے جائز کہا اور بعض نے ناجائز کہا ہے۔ پس جن حضرات نے اس کی تنہا بیع کو جائز کہا ہے ان کے نزدیک یہ سبزہ زمین کے تابع ہو کر زمین کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ یہ سبزہ بائع کا ہوگا اور زمین مشتری کی ہوگی اور جن حضرات نے اس کی تنہا بیچ کو نا جائز کہا ہے ان کے نزدیک یہ سبزہ زمین کی بیچ میں داخل ہوگا اور زمین اور سبز ہ دونوں مشتری کے لئے ہوں گے۔ ولايـد خل الزرع .... الخ کھیتی لگی ہوئی زمین اور پھل لگے ہوئے درخت کی بیج کے مختلف الفاظ کا ذکر ہے۔ بعض صورتوں میں کھیتی اور پھل بیچ میں داخل ہو جاتے ہیں اور بعض صورتوں میں داخل نہیں ہوتے تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ کھیتی لگی ہوئی زمین اور پھل لگے ہوئے درخت کی بیع میں چار قسم کے الفاظ بولے جا سکتے ہیں۔
ا۔ مالک نے کہا بعت الارض یا بعت الشجرۃ یعنی میں نے زمین فروخت کی یا میں نے درخت فروخت کیا۔ اس سے زیادہ کچھ نہیں کہا۔ اس کا حکم سابق میں گذر چکا کہ زمین کی بیچ میں کھیتی اور درخت کی بیچ میں پھل داخل نہ ہوں گے۔
-۲ مالک نے کہا بعث بحقوقها ومرافقها یعنی میں نے زمین یا درخت کو اس کے حقوق اور اس کے مرافق کے ساتھ فروخت کیا۔ ۔ اس نے کہا بعت بكل قليل وكثير هوله فيها ومنها من حقوقها او من مرافقها یعنی میں نے ہر قلیل و کثیر کے ساتھ جو اس مبیع میں یا مبیع سے اس کے حقوق یا مرافق میں سے ہو فروخت کیا۔
بعت بكل قليل وكثير هوله فيها - یعنی میں نے ہر قلیل و کثیر کے ساتھ جو اس میچ میں ہے فروخت کیا اور من حقوقها یا من مرافقها نہیں کہا۔
دوسری اور تیسری صورت میں زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی اور درخت کی بیع میں پھل داخل نہیں ہوں گے ۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں حقوق یا مرافق کا لفظ مذکور ہے۔ اور کھیتی اور پھل دونوں نہ حقوق میں سے ہیں اور نہ مرافق میں سے ہیں۔ اس لئے کہ حقوق مبیع کے ان توابع کو کہتے ہیں جو مبیع کے لئے ضروری ہوں اور مبیع ہی کی وجہ سے مقصود ہوں ۔ جیسے سینچے کا پانی اور راستہ اور مرافق وہ توابع ہیں جو مشفع ہوں اور مبیع کے ساتھ مختص ہوں جیسے وضو خانہ ، باورچی خانہ، اور پانی نکلنے کا راستہ مکان کے مرافق میں سے ہیں ۔ پس چونکہ کھیتی زمین کے حقوق اور مرافق میں سے نہیں ہے۔ اور پھل درخت کے حقوق اور مرافق میں سے نہیں ہیں۔ اس لئے لفظ حقوق یا لفظ مرافق ذکر کرنے سے زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی اور درخت کی بیع میں پھل داخل نہ ہوں گے ۔ بلکہ یہ دونوں بائع کے لئے ہوں گے۔ اور چوتھی صورت میں یعنی جب لفظ حقوق اور مرافق ذکر نہ کیا ہو تو کھیتی اور پھل دونوں بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے ۔ کیونکہ جب بائع نے بعث بكل قليل وكثير هوله فيها کہا تو عموم لفظ کی وجہ سے کھیتی زمین کی بیع میں اور پھل درخت کی بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے لیکن یہ اس وقت ہے جبکہ کھیتی زمین میں کھڑی ہو اور پھل درخت پر لگے ہوں۔ چنانچہ اگر پھل ٹوٹے ہوئے اور کھیتی کٹی ہوئی رکھی ہے تو بكل قليل وكثير کہنے کے باوجود بھی کھیتی اور پھل بیع میں داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ اس وقت یہ رکھے ہوئے سامان کے مانند ہے اور رکھا ہوا سامان دوسرے کے تابع ہو کر بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوتا ۔ اسی طرح کٹی ہوئی کھیتی اور ٹوٹے ہوئے پھل بھی زمین اور درخت کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوں گے ۔ ہاں اگر صراحتہ ذکر کر دیا ہو تو کٹی ہوئی کھیتی اور ٹوٹے ہوئے پھل زمین اور درخت کی بیچ میں داخل ہو جائیں گے اور دونوں مشتری کے لئے ہوں گے۔
Ashrāf al-Hidāyah commentary on Hidāyah volume eight, book of sales. There is disagreement about the validity of sale before eligibility. And with mention of rights and appurtenances, crops and fruits are not included, because these two are not from rights and appurtenances. And if the seller said 'with every small and great that is in this sale item or from it from its rights or from its appurtenances', still crops and fruits are not included, because of the proof we mentioned. And if he did not say 'from its rights or from its appurtenances', then crops and fruits become included in the sale. As for plucked fruits and cut crops, they are not included except with explicit mention, because they are like movable goods. Explanation: The scenario is that a person sold his land while he had planted seeds in it and the seeds have not yet germinated. Then the seeds are not included in the sale of the land; rather the seeds belong to the seller and the land belongs to the buyer. The proof is that the seeds are stored in the land, and a stored item is not included in the sale, just as goods stored in a house are not included in the sale of the house. In some fatwa books it is stated that if the seeds have rotted, then they become subsidiary to the land and are included in the sale, belonging to the buyer, because the sale of rotten grain alone is not valid, so it becomes subsidiary to the land. Yes, if it has not rotted, then the seeds are not included in the sale and belong to the seller. And if the seeds sprouted and tiny shoots emerged, but they have no market value, some scholars opine that the greenery is not included in the sale of the land, while others opine that it is included. According to the first view, the greenery belongs to the seller, and according to the second, to the buyer. The author of Hidāyah says this difference is based on another difference: whether such greenery that cannot be cut with sickles nor can animals grasp it with their lips (i.e., the shoots have just emerged from the ground) can be sold alone or not. Some said it is valid, some said invalid. Those who said its sole sale is valid hold that the greenery is not subsidiary to the land and is not included, so it belongs to the seller. Those who said its sole sale is invalid hold that it is subsidiary and included, so both go to the buyer. 'ولا يدخل الزرع' etc. There is mention of different phrases for land with standing crops and tree with fruits. In some cases crops and fruits are included, in some not. The detail: Four types of wording may be used: 1) The owner said 'bactu al-ard' or 'bactu al-shajara' — I sold the land or the tree, and nothing more. The ruling has passed earlier that crops are not included in sale of land, nor fruits in sale of tree. 2) The owner said 'bactu bi-huquqiha wa marafiqiha' — I sold the land or tree with its rights and appurtenances. 3) He said 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fiha wa minha min huquqiha aw min marafiqiha' — I sold with every small and great that is in it or from it from its rights or appurtenances. 4) 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin huwa fiha' — I sold with every small and great that is in it, without mentioning rights or appurtenances. In the second and third cases, crops and fruits are not included because the word 'rights' or 'appurtenances' is mentioned, and crops and fruits are neither rights nor appurtenances. Rights are those accessories necessary for the sold item and intended because of it, like irrigation water and path. Appurtenances are those accessories that are attached and specific to the sold item, like a washroom, kitchen, and drainage path of a house. Since crops are not rights or appurtenances of land, and fruits are not of trees, mentioning them does not include crops or fruits; they remain the seller's. In the fourth case, when rights and appurtenances are not mentioned, then both crops and fruits are included because of the general word 'every small and great that is in it', but this is when the crops are standing and fruits are attached. If fruits are plucked and crops are cut, even with 'bactu bi-kulli qalilin wa kathirin', they are not included because they are like stored goods, and stored goods are not included as subsidiary. However, if explicitly mentioned, then cut crops and plucked fruits become included and belong to the buyer.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah provides a detailed ruling on when standing crops (زرع) and attached fruits (ثمر) are included in the sale of land and trees. The author begins by stating a general principle: if the seller mentions 'rights' (حقوق) or 'appurtenances' (مرافق), crops and fruits are not included because they do not fall under those categories. If the seller does not mention them, then crops and fruits are included, but only if they are still attached (standing or hanging). Plucked fruits and cut crops are treated like movable goods and require explicit mention to be included. The commentary then elaborates on specific scenarios involving seeds that have not yet sprouted. The ruling here is clear: seeds that are simply stored in the ground (not yet sprouted) are like goods stored in a house and are not included in the sale of the land. However, if the seeds have rotted, they become subsidiary to the land and are included because they are no longer independently saleable. A dispute is noted among later scholars regarding seeds that have sprouted but are so tiny that they have no market value and cannot be cut or grazed. This dispute is traced back to a disagreement on whether such tiny sprouts can be sold independently. If they can be sold independently, they are not subsidiary and belong to the seller; if not, they become subsidiary and belong to the buyer. Finally, the passage systematically categorizes four types of wording in the sale of land with crops or trees with fruits, summarizing the rulings for each case. The key legal principle is the distinction between 'rights' (indispensable accessories like water rights) and 'appurtenances' (non-essential but attached features like outbuildings), and that crops and fruits fall into neither category unless explicitly included through general wording like 'بكل قليل وكثير' (with everything small and great).

Key Terms
تابع (tābiʿ): That which follows the principal (متبوع) in legal rulings; a subsidiary that is automatically included in the sale of the principal under certain conditions, e.g., when it cannot be sold independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi law, if seeds planted in land have rotted before the sale, are they included in the sale of the land?
A) No, because they are still stored goods.
B) Yes, because rotted seeds cannot be sold independently, so they become subsidiary.
C) Only if the buyer explicitly asks for them.
D) No, unless the seller mentions 'rights and appurtenances'.

Key Takeaway
Seeds stored in the ground are like goods in a house and not included in the sale; but if they rot or sprout, their inclusion is governed by independent saleability.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of Fruit Before and After Ripeness: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on fruit sale before ripeness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before ripeness, explain the definition of بدو صلاح according to both schools, and compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on this issue.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit selling unripe fruit as valuable property; Shafi'is require ripeness.

This lesson examines the validity of selling fruit before ripeness, a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Hanafi school, relying on the principle that fruit is valuable property (مال متقوم), allows its sale even before ripeness, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts it until the fruit shows signs of ripening. Understanding this dispute illuminates how different definitions of 'value' and 'ripeness' affect contract validity.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from al-Quduri's matn: sale of fruit is valid whether ripeness has appeared or not, because it is valuable property (مال متقوم).
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that sale before ripeness (defined as reddening/sweetening) is not valid.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i argument: unripe fruit is not fully valuable, may spoil, and cannot be sold until it is fit for consumption.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal: fruit has current or potential benefit (e.g., as fodder), so it is مال متقوم and a valid subject of sale.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical consequence: Hanafis allow sale of unripe fruit; Shafi'is require ripening before sale. This affects farmers, traders, and contract timing.

وصلاح سے پہلے اور بعد پھل کی بیع جائز ہے
قال و من باع ثمرة لم يبد صلاحها اوقد بداجاز البيع لانه مال متقوم اما لكونه منتفعا به فى الحال اوفى. الثاني وقد قيل لا يجوز قبل ان يبد وصلاحها والاول اصح وعلى المشترى قطعها في الحال تفريغا للملك البائع و هذا اذا اشتراها مطلقا او بشرط القطع
ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا کہ جس نے ایسے پھل فروخت کئے جن کی صلاح ظاہر نہیں ہوئی یا صلاح ظاہر ہوگئی تو بیع جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مال متقوم ہے یا تو اس لئے کہ وہ فی الحال قابل انتقاع ہے یا آئندہ زمانہ میں قابل انتفاع ہوگا ۔ اور کہا گیا کہ صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے۔ اور قول اول اصح ہے اور بائع کی ملک کو فارغ کرنے کے لئے مشتری پر ان پھلوں کو فی الحال توڑنا واجب ہے اور جواز اس وقت ہے جبکہ ان پھلوں کو مطلقاً یا توڑ لینے کی شرط پر خریدا ہو۔
تشریح صلاح ظاہر ہونا ہمارے نزدیک یہ ہے کہ پھل آندھی وغیرہ کی آفت اور پالے وغیرہ کے فساد سے محفوظ ہو جائیں اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک گدرانا اور مٹھاس کا شروع ہو جانا صلاح کا ظاہر ہو جانا ہے۔
صورت مسئلہ درختوں پر پھلوں کے بیچنے کی دو صورتیں ہیں ایک ظہور ثمر سے پہلے دوم ظہور ثمر کے بعد ۔ پہلی صورت تو بالا تفاق
And sale of fruit before and after ripeness is permissible.
He said: 'Whoever sells fruit whose ripeness has not yet appeared, or whose ripeness has appeared, the sale is valid because it is valuable property (māl mutaqawwim), either because it is beneficial in the present or will be in the future. And it has been said: It is not permissible before ripeness appears, but the first opinion is more correct (al-awwal aṣaḥ). And upon the buyer is to cut them immediately to vacate the seller's ownership. And this is when he buys them absolutely or with the condition of cutting.'
Translation: Al-Quduri said: Whoever sells fruit whose ripeness has not appeared, or ripeness has appeared, the sale is valid because it is valuable property, either because it is currently beneficial or will be in the future. And it was said that it is not permissible before ripeness appears, but the first statement is more correct. And it is obligatory for the buyer to cut them immediately to vacate the seller's ownership, and the permissibility is when he bought them absolutely or with the condition of cutting.
Explanation: The appearance of ripeness, according to us (Hanafis), is that the fruit becomes safe from calamities like wind and frost. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, the appearance of ripeness is the beginning of reddening and sweetening.
The scenario: There are two cases of selling fruit on trees: first, before the appearance of the fruit (i.e., before flowering), second, after the appearance of the fruit. The first case is unanimously...

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Hidayah deals with the ruling on selling fruit before and after ripeness (بدو صلاح). The matn from al-Quduri states that sale of fruit is valid whether ripeness has appeared or not, because it is valuable property (مال متقوم) — it has current or potential benefit. However, there is a known opposing view (from Imam Shafi'i) that sale before ripeness is not valid. The Hanafi ruling is that it is valid, and this is the stronger position (الاول اصح). Once sold, the buyer must cut the fruit immediately ('علی المشتری قطعها فی الحال) to vacate ownership of the seller and prevent the fruit from remaining on the tree after sale, which could lead to issues. This condition applies when the fruit is bought unconditionally or with the condition of cutting. The commentary then explains the Hanafi definition of 'بدو صلاح' (appearance of ripeness): it is when the fruit becomes safe from natural calamities such as wind and frost. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i defines it as the beginning of ripening (reddening or sweetening). This difference leads to practical consequences: under the Shafi'i view, fruit that is not yet sweet or red cannot be sold before that point, while under the Hanafi view, it can be sold as long as it is valuable. The passage ends noting two scenarios: sale before the appearance of fruit (presumably before blossoms) and after appearance; the first scenario is unanimously valid, but the text is cut off. The overall ruling is a clear example of Hanafi leniency in sales, recognizing the property value of unripe fruit.

Key Terms
بدو صلاح (badw al-ṣalāḥ): The appearance of ripeness. According to Hanafis: when fruit becomes safe from natural calamities like wind and frost. According to Shafi'is: when the fruit starts to redden or sweeten.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and can be sold, having both material value and lawful usability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is the sale of fruit on a tree valid?
A) Only after ripeness appears (as defined by Hanafis: safety from calamities).
B) Only after the fruit has fully ripened (turned sweet).
C) Both before and after the appearance of ripeness.
D) Only after the fruit has been cut from the tree.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis permit selling unripe fruit because it is valuable property (مال متقوم), with the condition that the buyer must cut it immediately.

Inclusion of Crops and Fruit in Sale: The Hanafi Rule on Contractual Wording
Explains when crops/fruit are included in sale based on contractual wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether standing crops or hanging fruit are included in the sale of land or trees based on the wording used, and distinguish between attached and detached items.

Core Idea
General words include attached produce; specifying 'rights' or 'facilities' excludes it; detached items are never automatically included.

When a landowner sells his land, does the buyer automatically get the standing crops? What about harvested stacks? The answer depends on how the contract is worded and whether the crops are attached. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of appurtenances (huquq and marafiq) and the default rules for inclusion in sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
The passage describes four cases based on the seller's wording and the state of crops/fruit. List each scenario and its outcome.
Focus on the wording: general (bikull qalil wa kathir), rights (huquq), facilities (marafiq), or no mention.
2
Define huquq and marafiq
Understand the Hanafi definitions: huquq are necessary appurtenances like water rights; marafiq are beneficial appurtenances like a kitchen.
These are specific legal categories, not all contents.
3
Apply definitions to crops and fruit
Recognize that standing crops/hanging fruit are not huquq or marafiq; they are tawabi' (attached produce) that follow the principal.
They are considered part of the land/tree when attached.
4
Determine inclusion based on wording
If general wording is used, attached produce is included. If huquq or marafiq is specified, exclude because crops/fruit don't fall under those categories.
General wording is comprehensive; specific wording restricts.
5
Handle the detachment exception
If crops are harvested or fruit plucked, they are like stored goods and not included even with general wording, unless explicitly mentioned.
Attachment is key to automatic inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعت بكل قليل وكثير هوله فيها - یعنی میں نے ہر قلیل و کثیر کے ساتھ جو اس میچ میں ہے فروخت کیا اور من حقوقها یا من مرافقها نہیں کہا۔ دوسری اور تیسری صورت میں زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی اور درخت کی بیع میں پھل داخل نہیں ہوں گے ۔ کیونکہ ان دونوں صورتوں میں حقوق یا مرافق کا لفظ مذکور ہے۔ اور کھیتی اور پھل دونوں نہ حقوق میں سے ہیں اور نہ مرافق میں سے ہیں۔ اس لئے کہ حقوق مبیع کے ان توابع کو کہتے ہیں جو مبیع کے لئے ضروری ہوں اور مبیع ہی کی وجہ سے مقصود ہوں ۔ جیسے سینچے کا پانی اور راستہ اور مرافق وہ توابع ہیں جو مشفع ہوں اور مبیع کے ساتھ مختص ہوں جیسے وضو خانہ ، باورچی خانہ، اور پانی نکلنے کا راستہ مکان کے مرافق میں سے ہیں ۔ پس چونکہ کھیتی زمین کے حقوق اور مرافق میں سے نہیں ہے۔ اور پھل درخت کے حقوق اور مرافق میں سے نہیں ہیں۔ اس لئے لفظ حقوق یا لفظ مرافق ذکر کرنے سے زمین کی بیع میں کھیتی اور درخت کی بیع میں پھل داخل نہ ہوں گے ۔ بلکہ یہ دونوں بائع کے لئے ہوں گے۔ اور چوتھی صورت میں یعنی جب لفظ حقوق اور مرافق ذکر نہ کیا ہو تو کھیتی اور پھل دونوں بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے ۔ کیونکہ جب بائع نے بعث بكل قليل وكثير هوله فيها کہا تو عموم لفظ کی وجہ سے کھیتی زمین کی بیع میں اور پھل درخت کی بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے لیکن یہ اس وقت ہے جبکہ کھیتی زمین میں کھڑی ہو اور پھل درخت پر لگے ہوں۔ چنانچہ اگر پھل ٹوٹے ہوئے اور کھیتی کٹی ہوئی رکھی ہے تو بكل قليل وكثير کہنے کے باوجود بھی کھیتی اور پھل بیع میں داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ اس وقت یہ رکھے ہوئے سامان کے مانند ہے اور رکھا ہوا سامان دوسرے کے تابع ہو کر بیع میں داخل نہیں ہوتا ۔ اسی طرح کئی ہوئی کھیتی اور ٹوٹے ہوئے پھل بھی زمین اور درخت کی بیع میں داخل نہ ہوں گے ۔ ہاں اگر صراحتہ ذکر کر دیا ہو تو کئی ہوئی کھیتی اور ٹوٹے ہوئے پھل زمین اور درخت کی بیع میں داخل ہو جائیں گے اور دونوں مشتری کے لئے ہوں گے۔
I sold with every small and large thing that is in it - meaning I sold with every small and large thing that is in this [land/asset], and I did not say 'from its rights' or 'from its facilities'. In the second and third scenarios, crops are not included in the sale of land, and fruit are not included in the sale of trees, because in both these scenarios the word 'rights' or 'facilities' is mentioned. And crops and fruit are neither from the rights nor from the facilities. Because rights refer to those appurtenances of the sold item that are necessary for it and are intended because of the sold item itself, such as irrigation water and pathway. And facilities are those appurtenances that are beneficial and specific to the sold item, such as a washroom, kitchen, and drainage path as part of a house's facilities. Therefore, since crops are not among the rights or facilities of land, and fruit are not among the rights or facilities of trees, with the mention of 'rights' or 'facilities', crops and fruit are not included in the sale. Rather, both belong to the seller. In the fourth scenario, i.e., when the words 'rights' and 'facilities' are not mentioned, both crops and fruit are included in the sale, because when the seller says 'I sold with every small and large thing that is in it', due to the generality of the word, crops are included in the sale of land and fruit in the sale of trees. But this is when the crops are standing in the field and the fruit are attached to the tree. However, if the fruit is plucked and the crops are harvested, even with saying 'with every small and large thing', the crops and fruit are not included in the sale, because at that time it is like stored goods, and stored goods are not included as a subordinate of another in the sale. Similarly, harvested crops and plucked fruit are not included in the sale of land and trees. However, if explicitly mentioned, then harvested crops and plucked fruit are included in the sale of land and trees, and both belong to the buyer.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school that elaborates on the interpretation of contractual wording in the sale of land and trees, specifically regarding the inclusion of crops and fruit. The author presents four scenarios based on the seller's language. First, if the seller says 'I sold with every small and large thing that is in it' (bikull qalil wa kathir), then standing crops and attached fruit are included automatically due to the generality of the phrase. Second and third, if the seller specifies 'rights' (huquq) or 'facilities' (marafiq), crops and fruit are not included because they are not considered legal rights or facilities; rights are necessary appurtenances like water rights or access, while facilities are amenities like a bathroom or kitchen. The Hanafi school distinguishes these as integral to the property but not the produce itself. Fourth, if no such wording is used, the default inclusion depends on whether the crops/fruit are attached; if detached, they are like stored goods and not included unless explicitly mentioned. The ruling emphasizes that the intention of the parties and the state of the items at the time of sale determine inclusion, following the principle that appurtenances (tawabi') are included with the principal (asl) in sale. The Hanafi position here is that general terms include standing crops and hanging fruit, while specific restrictive terms exclude them, reflecting the school's reliance on contractual language and custom ('urf).

Key Terms
حقوق (huquq): Appurtenances that are necessary for the sold item and intended because of it, such as irrigation water and pathway.
مرافق (marafiq): Beneficial appurtenances specific to the property, like a washroom or kitchen.
توابع (tawabi'): Appurtenances that follow the principal item in sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the wording is general and includes all contents.
B) Yes, because crops always follow the land.
C) No, because harvested crops are like stored goods and not appurtenances.
D) No, because the seller did not explicitly mention crops.

Key Takeaway
General wording includes attached crops and fruit; specifying 'rights' or 'facilities' excludes them; detached items require explicit mention.

Sale of Unripe Fruit: The Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on selling unripe fruit with Hadith reinterpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before ripeness, identify the opposing proofs, explain the Hanafi response to the hadith prohibition, and apply the ruling to a scenario.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit selling unripe fruit because it is valuable property, interpreting the prophetic prohibition as applying to conditional or forward sales.

Can you sell fruit that hasn't ripened yet? The Hanafi school answers yes, while three other major schools say no. This lesson unpacks the dispute, examining how the Hanafi school reconciles its position with a Prophetic hadith that appears to forbid such sales. We will follow the argument from al-Quduri's matn and its elaboration in al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on selling fruit before ripeness
Identify what al-Quduri says: the sale is valid (jā'iz) whether ripeness has appeared or not. The core reason is that fruit is considered valuable property (māl mutaqawwam) either because it is currently beneficial or will be so in the future.
The buyer must cut the fruit immediately after purchase to vacate the seller's ownership of the trees.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proofs
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold the sale is invalid before ripeness. Their first proof is a hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) prohibited selling fruit before ripeness. Their second proof is that unripe fruit is not māl mutaqawwam because it lacks immediate benefit.
These schools define 'badw al-salah' as the beginning of physical ripening (color and sweetness).
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the hadith
The Hanafi school offers two interpretations: (1) the hadith prohibits selling with a condition to leave the fruit on the tree (making it a conditional sale, which is invalid due to gharar), and (2) the hadith actually prohibits salam (forward sale) where delivery is deferred. The narrator's comment 'if Allah destroys the fruit, how could you take your brother's wealth?' applies only when delivery is future, not immediate.
Thus the hadith is not a proof against an immediate spot sale of unripe fruit.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
According to Hanafis, a buyer can purchase unripe fruit on the tree and cut it immediately—the sale is valid. According to the other schools, such a sale is invalid (bāṭil) and the contract is void.
If the fruit is sold with a condition to leave it on the tree, even Hanafis consider the sale invalid (fāsid).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author of al-Hidayah states that the first view (permissibility in both states) is more correct (al-awwal aṣaḥḥ), following al-Quduri and the majority of Hanafi scholars. This is the operative position for fatwa.
The authority weight is ẓāhir al-riwāyah from the ṣāḥibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من باع ثمرة لم يبد صلاحها اوقد بداجاز البيع لانه مال متقوم اما لكونه منتفعا به فى الحال اوفى. الثاني وقد قيل لا يجوز قبل ان يبد وصلاحها والاول اصح وعلى المشترى قطعها في الحال تفريغا للملك البائع و هذا اذا اشتراها مطلقا او بشرط القطع ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا کہ جس نے ایسے پھل فروخت کئے جن کی صلاح ظاہر نہیں ہوئی یا صلاح ظاہر ہوگئی تو بیع جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ مال متقوم ہے یا تو اس لئے کہ وہ فی الحال توڑنا واجب ہے اور جواز اس وقت ہے جبکہ ان پھلوں کو مطلقاً یا توڑ لینے کی شرط پر خریدا ہو۔ تشریح صلاح ظاہر ہونا ہمارے نزدیک یہ ہے کہ پھل آندھی وغیرہ کی آفت اور پالے وغیرہ کے فساد سے محفوظ ہو جائیں اور پھل صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے بھی متقوم ہیں اور صلاح ظاہر ہونے کے بعد بھی متقوم ہیں اور مال متقوم کی بیع جائز ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے مذکورہ دونوں صورتوں میں پھلوں کی بیع جائز ہوگی۔ امام شافعی و غیرہ کی طرف سے اس لئے مشتری پر فوری طور سے پھلوں کو توڑنا واجب ہوگا۔ تا کہ اپنی ملک سے بائع کی ملک یعنی درختوں کو فارغ کرے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ درخت پر پھلوں کی بیع کا جواز اس وقت ہے جبکہ پھلوں کو بغیر کسی شرط کے خریدا ہو یعنی ان کو نہ توڑنے کی شرط لگائی ہو اور نہ چھوڑنے کی شرط لگائی ہو یا توڑنے کی شرط کے ساتھ خریدا ہو۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And whoever sells fruit whose ripeness has not appeared or whose ripeness has appeared, the sale is valid, because it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwam), either because it is currently beneficial or will be in the future. And it has been said that it is not permissible before its ripeness appears, and the first view is more correct. And it is obligatory upon the buyer to cut them immediately to vacate the seller's ownership. And this is when he buys them absolutely or with the condition of cutting. Explanation: According to us (Hanafis), the appearance of ripeness (badw al-salah) is that the fruit become safe from calamities like wind and frost. According to Imam Shafi'i, the beginning of coloration and sweetness is the appearance of ripeness. The issue of selling fruit on trees has two scenarios: before the appearance of the fruit (i.e., after pollination) and after the appearance of fruit. The first scenario is unanimously invalid. In the second scenario, after ripeness appears, it is unanimously permissible. Before ripeness appears, it is permissible according to us, but according to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad, it is impermissible. In summary, according to us, selling fruit on trees is permissible both after ripeness appears and before it appears. According to the three Imams, selling before ripeness is not permissible. This is the view of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. But the first view (that it is permissible in both states) is more correct. The proof of Imam Shafi'i and others is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling fruit before ripeness appears. Their second proof is that sale is specific to valuable property (mal mutaqawwam), and before ripeness, fruit are not mutaqawwam. Therefore, sale before ripeness is impermissible. The Hanafi proof is our argument that fruit are mutaqawwam, because if ripeness appears, they are currently beneficial; if ripeness has not appeared, they will become beneficial in the future. Whatever is beneficial is mutaqawwam. So fruit are mutaqawwam both before and after ripeness, and sale of mutaqawwam property is valid. Therefore, selling fruit in both scenarios is valid. The response to the hadith presented by Shafi'i and others is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling fruit before ripeness in the case when a condition is made to leave them on the tree. And the sale with the condition of leaving on the tree is invalid, as will come under the next issue. Or the meaning is that the Prophet prohibited making a salam (forward) sale before ripeness, and the indication is that the Prophet said: 'Do you see that if Allah destroys the fruit, how could one of you take his brother's wealth?' And this applies only in salam sale because in salam, the delivery of the sold item (muslam fih) is not immediate but deferred. Therefore, the hadith is not a proof for Shafi'i and others. Imam al-Quduri said: Since the sale of fruit is permissible according to us both before and after ripeness, it is obligatory upon the buyer to cut them immediately to vacate the seller's ownership of the trees. The author of al-Hidayah says: The permissibility of selling fruit on trees is when they are bought without any condition, i.e., neither with a condition not to cut nor a condition to leave them, or with a condition to cut.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of fruit on trees before ripeness (badw al-salah). The Hanafi school (according to al-Quduri and supported by al-Hidayah) holds that such a sale is valid because fruit is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwam) either immediately or in the future. The opposing schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) argue that it is invalid based on a Prophetic prohibition and the argument that unripe fruit is not mutaqawwam. The Hanafi response to the hadith is twofold: first, the prohibition applies only when a condition to leave the fruit on the tree is made (which renders the sale invalid due to gharar), and second, the hadith actually prohibits salam (forward) sale where delivery is deferred, not a spot sale. The Hanafi rely on the principle that the subject matter must be beneficial, and unripe fruit has potential benefit, so it is mutaqawwam. The passage also clarifies the definition of 'badw al-salah': for Hanafis, it means the fruit has become safe from natural calamities; for Shafi'i, it means the fruit has started to ripen (color/sweetness). The practical ruling: the buyer must cut the fruit immediately after purchase to vacate the seller's ownership of the trees. The sale is only valid if the fruit is bought without any condition to leave them (which would cause fasad) or with a condition to cut them.

Key Terms
بدو صلاح (badw al-salah): The state when fruit becomes safe from natural calamities (wind, frost) according to Hanafis; start of ripeness (color/sweetness) according to Shafi'i.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwam): Property that is valuable and legally recognized as having permissible utility, thus eligible for sale.
بیع سلم (bay' salam): A forward sale where payment is made immediately but delivery is deferred to a future date.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the sale of fruit on trees before ripeness (badw al-salah) considered valid?
A) Because the fruit becomes ownerless and anyone can sell it.
B) Because the fruit is mutaqawwam either currently or potentially, and the hadith prohibition is interpreted as applying to salam or conditional sales.
C) Because the fruit is not considered property, so no rules apply.
D) Because the seller retains ownership until ripening.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between a spot sale of unripe fruit (valid) and a conditional or forward sale (invalid), resolving the apparent hadith conflict through textual interpretation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Selling Fruits Before and After Maturity: Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Presents Hanafi permissibility vs. Shafi'i/Maliki/Hanbali prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling fruits before ripeness and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Fruit sale before ripeness is Hanafi-permissible because the fruit is mutaqawwim; the hadith of prohibition applies only to conditional or salam sales.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: the sale of unripe fruit on trees. The Hanafi school permits it, while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools prohibit it. The disagreement hinges on the definition of mutaqawwim property and the correct interpretation of a prophetic hadith. By the end, you will grasp the Hanafi reasoning and the practical implications for farmers and traders.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school permits selling fruit on trees both before and after the appearance of signs of ripeness (salah zahir). This is the mufta bihi position.
The author, Ashraf al-Hidayah, prefers permissibility in both states as 'more correct'.
2
Identify the opponent's view and proof
Opponents (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) prohibit sales before ripeness. Their main proof is a hadith forbidding such sales, and the argument that unripe fruit is not mutaqawwim (legally valuable property).
The hadith is often cited: 'Do not sell fruit until its ripeness is manifest' (various narrations).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Analyze the opponent's dalil: the hadith appears to be a general prohibition, and the requirement of mutaqawwim seems to exclude unripe fruit which cannot be immediately benefited from.
This step requires careful reading of the opponent's reasoning as presented in the text.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response has two parts: (1) The hadith is interpreted as referring to conditional sale (with a stipulation to leave the fruit on the tree) or to salam (forward) sale, not ordinary sale. (2) Unripe fruit is mutaqawwim because it can be benefited from in the future.
The hadith's own wording ('Do you not see that if Allah destroys the fruit...') supports the salam interpretation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Hanafi farmers can sell their crop in advance without waiting for ripeness, while other schools require waiting for signs of ripeness.
This difference affects cash flow, risk allocation, and market practices.

Source Text

امام شافعی وغیرہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ رسول اکرم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے پھلوں کی بیچ سے منع فرمایا ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیج مال متقوم کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے۔ اور صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے پھل متقوم نہیں ہوتے ۔ اس لئے صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے پھلوں کی بیع نا جائز ہے۔ احناف کی دلیل ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ پھل مال متقوم ہیں کیونکہ اگر صلاح ظاہر ہوگئی تو وہ فی الحال منتفع یہ ہیں اور اگر صلاح ظاہر نہیں ہوئی تو آئندہ چل کر منتفع بہ ہو جائیں گے اور جو چیز قابل انتفاع ہو وہ متقوم ہوتی ہے اس لئے پھل صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے بھی متقوم ہیں اور صلاح ظاہر ہونے کے بعد بھی متقوم ہیں اور مال متقوم کی بیع جائز ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے مذکورہ دونوں صورتوں میں پھلوں کی بیچ جائز ہوگی۔

It is not permissible, and in the second case, after the manifestation of ripeness (salah zahir) it is permissible by consensus, and before that, according to us (Hanafis) it is permissible, but according to Imam Malik, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Ahmad it is impermissible. But the first saying, i.e., permissibility in both states, is more correct. The proof of Imam Shafi'i and others is that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited selling fruits before the manifestation of ripeness. The second proof is that sale is specific to mutaqawwim property, and before the manifestation of ripeness, fruits are not mutaqawwim. Therefore, selling fruits before ripeness is impermissible. The Hanafi proof: Our proof is that fruits are mutaqawwim property because if ripeness has appeared, they are currently benefited from, and if ripeness has not appeared, they will be benefited from in the future. Whatever is capable of being benefited from is mutaqawwim. Therefore, fruits are mutaqawwim both before and after the manifestation of ripeness, and the sale of mutaqawwim property is valid. Hence, in both mentioned cases, the sale of fruits is valid. And the contextual indication is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Do you not see that if Allah destroys the fruit, how could any of you take the property of his brother?' This can only happen in the case of a salam sale, because in salam, the delivery of the muslam fihi is not immediate but later. In any case, it is understood from this that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited making a salam (forward sale) of fruits before ripeness. After this answer, this hadith will not be a proof for Imam Shafi'i and others.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of fruits on trees before and after they show signs of ripeness (salah zahir). The Hanafi position, stated first, is that such a sale is permissible both before and after ripeness. The opposing schools – Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanbali – hold that selling before ripeness is impermissible. The passage then presents the proofs of the opposition: a hadith prohibiting such sales (narrated in various collections) and the argument that unripe fruit is not mutaqawwim (valuable property in Islamic law). The Hanafi response refutes both: (1) The hadith is interpreted as referring either to a conditional sale where the fruit is left on the tree (which itself renders the sale invalid due to a separate legal principle) or to a salam (forward) sale, not an ordinary sale. The hadith's own wording supports the salam interpretation, as the hypothetical loss of fruit only matters if the payment was made in advance. (2) Unripe fruit is indeed mutaqawwim because it can still be benefited from (e.g., grazing animals, or future consumption), and Hanafi law defines mutaqawwim as anything capable of lawful benefit. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling of permissibility stands. The passage concludes that after this refutation, the hadith does not support the opponent's position.

Key Terms
صلاح ظاہر (salah zahir): The appearance of signs of ripeness in fruit, such as change of color or sweetness.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and permissible to benefit from according to Islamic law.
بیع سلم (bay' salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance and the commodity is delivered later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Hanafi school permits selling fruit before ripeness because the fruit is mutaqawwim and the hadith of prohibition is interpreted to apply to specific types of sales, not ordinary sales.

Stipulation to Leave Fruit on Tree: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ruling on conditional sale of fruit on tree with proof and istihsan exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit with a condition to leave it on the tree, and identify the difference between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on this issue.

Core Idea
A condition to leave fruit on the tree makes the sale fasid because it contradicts the contract's requirement of immediate delivery.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a common agricultural transaction: selling fruit with a stipulation that the buyer leaves the fruit on the tree until ripening. The text from al-Hidayah presents the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that such a sale is fasid, while Imam Muhammad permits it by istihsan due to custom. We will explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequence for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The source passage discusses the validity of a sale of fruit on a tree with a condition that the buyer leaves the fruit on the tree until ripening. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the sale fasid; Muhammad al-Shaybani allows it by istihsan when the fruit has completed its growth.
The condition is the key point of contention.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the sale is fasid because the condition is contrary to the muqtada (required legal effect) of the sale contract, which is immediate delivery and separation of the subject matter from the seller's property. Additionally, it may involve safqa fi safqa (two transactions in one) if the permission to leave is compensated (ijara) or gratuitous (ariyah).
Muqtada al-aqd: the contract necessitates delivery.
3
Explain the majority proof
The condition causes the buyer to occupy the seller's property (trees) with his own property (fruits) without proper consideration, leading to gharar. If compensation is given for leaving, it becomes an ijara within a sale, which is prohibited by the hadith on safqa fi safqa. Even if no compensation, it is an ariyah, still a second transaction.
The prohibition of safqa fi safqa is from the Prophet ﷺ.
4
Present Imam Muhammad's istihsan
Imam Muhammad allows the sale by istihsan when the fruit has completed its growth (i.e., no further increase expected) because of the widespread custom ('aadah) of such transactions. He considers that the condition does not cause significant uncertainty once growth is complete.
Istihsan is a departure from strict qiyas due to custom.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the sale is fasid. Custom does not override clear legal principles, and the majority view is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Thus, in practice, such a conditional sale is invalid.

Source Text

صاحب قدوری فرماتے ہیں کہ ہمارے نزدیک چونکہ پھلوں کی بیع صلاح ظاہر ہونے سے پہلے بھی جائز ہے اور صلاح ظاہر ہونے کے بعد بھی۔ اس لئے مشتری پر فوری طور سے پھلوں کو توڑنا واجب ہوگا۔ تا کہ اپنی ملک سے بائع کی ملک یعنی درختوں کو فارغ کرے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فر ماتے ہیں کہ درخت پر پھلوں کی بیع کا جواز اس وقت ہے جبکہ پھلوں کو بغیر کسی شرط کے خریدا ہو یعنی ان کو نہ توڑنے کی شرط لگائی ہو اور نہ چھوڑنے کی شرط لگائی ہو یا توڑنے کی شرط کے ساتھ خریدا ہو۔
پھل خرید کر درختوں پر پکنے کی شرط لگانے سے بیع فاسد ہے
وان شرط تركها على النخيل فسد البيع لانه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وهو شغل ملك الغير وهو صفقه في صفقة وهوا عارة او اجارة فى بيع وكذابيع الزرع بشرط الترك لما قلنا وكذا اذتنا هي عظمها عندابی حنيفة وابى يوسف لما قلنا واستحسنه محمد للعادة بخلاف ما اذالم يتناه عظمها لانه شرط فيه الجز المعدوم وهو الذى يزيد بمعنى من الارض او الشجر
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری نے یہ شرط کی ہو کہ ان کو درختوں پر چھوڑے گا تو بیع فاسد ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ شرط ایسی ہے جس کا عقد تقاضا نہیں کر ہے اور وہ دوسرے کی ملک کا مشغول رکھنا ہے یا ایک صفقہ میں دوسرا صفتہ ہونا اور وہ بیچ میں اعادہ ہے یا اجارہ ہے اور اسی طرح کھیتی کی زمین میں چھوڑ دینے کی شرط کے ساتھ اس وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی اور اسی طرح ابو حنیفہ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک اس وقت جبکہ ان بڑھاور پوری ہوگئی ہو اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم نے بیان کی اور امام محمد نے عادت کی وجہ سے اس کو استحسانا جائز رکھا ہے۔ برخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ پھلوں کی بڑھ اور پوری نہ ہوئی ہو۔ کیونکہ اس میں جزو معدوم کی شرط ہے اور یہ وہ ہے جو زمین یا درخت کی قوت نے دھے گا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہےکہ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ میں نے اس درخت کے پھل اس شرط پر خریدے کہ ان کو پکنے کے وقت تک درخت چھوڑ دوں گا تو یہ بیع فاسد ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ شرط مقتضی عقد کے خلاف ہے۔ کیونکہ عقد کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ معقود علیہ مشتری کے سپر د کرد جائے اور مشتری بائع کی ملک سے معقود علیہ کو جدا کر لے۔ اور اس شرط کے ساتھ ایسا نہیں ہے بلکہ مشتری نے اپنی ملک یعنی پھلوں کے ساتھ بالکع کی ملک یعنی درختوں کو مشغول کر رکھا ہے۔ پس مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف شرط لگانے کی وجہ سے بیج فاسد ہو جائے گا ۔ دوسرا دلیل یہ ہے کہ پھلوں کو درخت پر چھوڑنے کی شرط لگانے کی صورت میں ایک صفقہ کا دوسرے صفقہ میں ہونا لازم آئے گا کیونکہ درخت پھلوں کا چھوڑ نا بالعوض ہوگا یا با اعوض ہو گا اگر بالعوض ہے یعنی مشتری نے درخت پر پھلوں کو چھوڑنے کی اجرت مقرر کر دی ہے تو بیچ میں اجارہ داخل ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر بلا عوض ہے یعنی مشترکی نے کوئی اجرت مقرر نہیں کی توبیع میں اعادہ (عاربیڈ دینا ) داخل ہو جائے گا حالانکہ صفقه في صفقہ سے رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے منع فرمایا ہے اس لئے اس شرط کے ساتھ بیع فاسد ہو جائے گا اسی طرح اس صورت میں بیع فاسد ہوگی جبکہ کسی نے کھڑی کھیتی کو خریدا ہو۔ اور پکنے کے وقت تک کھیتی زمین میں چھوڑنے کی شرط ہو دلیل سابق میں گذ چکی کہ یہ شرط مقتضا عقد کے خلاف اور صفقہ فی صفیہ بھی لازم آتا ہے۔ یہاں ایک سوال ہے وہ یہ کہ صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ درخت پر با اعوض یا با اعوض پھلوں کو چھوڑنے کی شرط لگانے کی صورت میں صفقته فى صفقتہ لازم آتا ہے۔ حالانکہ یہ غلط ہے کیونکہ درختوں کو نہ اجارہ پر دینا جائز ہے اور نہ عاریہ دینا جائز ہے۔ پس جب درختوں اجارہ اور امارہ نا جائز ہے تو یہ صفحہ ہی نہ ہوگا اور جب یہ اللہ نہیں ہے تو صفقہ فی صفقہ کہاں سے لازم آئے گا ہاں زراعت کی صورت میں صلاحه فى صفق یہ لازم آئے گا ۔ کیونکہ زمین کا اجارہ اور امار و دونوں جائز ہیں۔ اب حاصل یہ ہوا کہ پھلوں کو درخت پر چھوڑ نے کی شرط کے گا۔ اور پر چھوڑنے ساتھ فساد بیچ اس لئے ہے کہ یہ شرط مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف ہے۔ اور کھیتی کو زمین میں چھوڑنے کی شرط کے ساتھ دو وجہوں سے بیع فاسہ ہے۔ ایک مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف ہونے کی وجہ سے دوم صفقہ فی ہفتہ کی وجہ سے۔ صفقة ماحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر پھلوں کی بڑھاور پوری ہوگئی یعنی ان کو جس قدر بڑھنا تھا اور موٹا ہونا تھا وہ ہو چکا اب ان کے مجھے میں کوئی اضافہ متوقع نہیں ہے تو ایسی صورت میں اگر پھلوں کو اس شرط کے ساتھ خریدا کہ پکنے تک درخت پر چھوڑے رکھے گا تو شیخین کے نزدیک اس صورت میں بھی بیع فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ شرط مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف ہے اور مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف شر
Sahib Quduri says that according to us, since the sale of fruits is permissible both before and after the appearance of ripeness, therefore it is obligatory for the buyer to immediately pick the fruits, so that he vacates the seller's property (the trees) from his own property. Sahib Hidayah says that the permissibility of selling fruits on the tree is when the fruits are bought without any condition, i.e., neither a condition of not picking nor a condition of leaving, or if bought with a condition of picking. If a condition is made to leave them on the tree until ripening, the sale is fasid. The Arabic text: 'And if he stipulates leaving them on the palm trees, the sale is invalid, because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it is occupying the property of another, and it is a transaction within a transaction, and it is either an ariyah (loan) or an ijara (hire) in a sale, and similarly the sale of crops with a condition of leaving, for what we have said, and similarly when their growth has completed according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, for what we have said, and Muhammad considered it permissible by istihsan due to custom, unlike when their growth has not completed, because it contains a condition of a non-existent part, which is what increases by the power of the earth or tree.' Translation: If the buyer stipulates that he will leave them on the trees, the sale is invalid because this condition is not required by the contract, and it is occupying the property of another, or it is a second transaction within one transaction, and it is an ariyah or an ijara in a sale. And similarly, the sale of crops with a condition of leaving, for the reason we mentioned. And similarly, in the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, when their growth has completed, for the reason we mentioned, but Muhammad allowed it by istihsan due to custom, unlike when their growth has not completed, because it contains a condition of a non-existent part, which is what will increase by the power of the earth or tree. Explanation: The case is that if someone says, 'I bought these fruits on the condition that I will leave them on the tree until they ripen,' then this sale is fasid. The proof is that this condition is contrary to the requirement of the contract because the contract requires that the subject matter be delivered to the buyer and that the buyer separate it from the seller's property. With this condition, this does not happen; rather, the buyer occupies the seller's property (the trees) with his own property (the fruits). Therefore, due to stipulating a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract, the sale becomes fasid. The second proof is that stipulating the condition of leaving fruits on the tree necessitates a second transaction within one transaction because leaving the fruits on the tree will either be for consideration or without consideration. If for consideration, i.e., the buyer fixes a rent for leaving the fruits on the tree, then an ijara enters into the sale. If without consideration, i.e., the buyer does not fix any rent, then an ariyah enters into the sale. But the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited 'a transaction within a transaction' (safqa fi safqa). So with this condition, the sale becomes fasid. Similarly, the sale is fasid if someone buys standing crops with the condition of leaving them in the ground until ripening. The proof is the same. There is a question: Sahib Hidayah said that in the case of leaving fruits on a tree for consideration or without consideration, it would necessitate safqa fi safqa. But this is wrong because it is not permissible to give trees on ijara or ariyah, so there is no such transaction, and thus safqa fi safqa does not arise. However, in the case of crops, safqa fi safqa does arise because leasing and loaning land are both permissible. Now the conclusion: the invalidity of sale with the condition of leaving on trees is because the condition is contrary to the requirement of the contract. And the sale with the condition of leaving crops in the land is invalid due to two reasons: firstly, because it is contrary to the requirement of the contract, and secondly, because of safqa fi safqa. Sahib Hidayah says that if the growth of the fruits has completed, meaning they have grown as much as they would and have become large, and no further increase is expected, then in this case, if the fruits are bought with the condition of leaving them on the tree until ripening, according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) the sale is still fasid, because the condition is contrary to the requirement of the contract. Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a sale of fruits with a stipulation that the buyer will leave them on the tree until ripening. It begins with a general rule from Sahib Quduri that the buyer must pick the fruits immediately to vacate the seller's property. Sahib Hidayah then clarifies that the sale of fruit on trees is only permissible when no condition is attached to leave them, or if the condition is to pick them. If the buyer stipulates to leave them, the sale is fasid (invalid). The reason given in the Arabic text (from Hidayah) is that it is a condition not required by the contract, it occupies another's property, it amounts to a safqa fi safqa (two transactions in one), and it involves an ariyah (loan) or ijara (hire) within a sale. Similarly for the sale of crops. The passage then discusses the case when the fruit's growth has completed (i.e., they have reached full size but are not yet ripe). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the sale fasid even then, because the condition is still contrary to the contract. However, Imam Muhammad allows it by istihsan due to custom (people commonly do this). This is the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The passage then explains the reasoning in Urdu: the condition contradicts the requirement of the contract because the buyer keeps his property (fruits) on the seller's property (trees). Additionally, it creates a second transaction (safqa fi safqa) because the permission to leave the fruits is either an ijara (if rent is paid) or an ariyah (if free). A hadith prohibits safqa fi safqa. A question is raised that for trees, ijara and ariyah are not valid, so safqa fi safqa does not occur. The answer is that for fruits, the invalidity is only due to the condition being contrary to the contract, not due to safqa fi safqa. But for crops, both reasons apply because leasing land is valid. The passage concludes with the ruling of the two shaykhs that the sale is fasid even if growth has completed.

Key Terms
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is invalid due to a defect in the consideration or condition, but can potentially be rectified if the defect is removed (e.g., by removing the condition).
صفحہ فے صفجه (safqa fi safqa): Two transactions combined into one contract, which is prohibited by hadith. Here, it refers to the sale including either an ariyah or ijara.
مقتضائے عقد (muqtada al-aqd): The necessary legal effect of a contract, which for a sale is the transfer of ownership and immediate delivery.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from an established analogy in favor of a more suitable ruling due to custom, necessity, or other evidence.
جزو معدوم (juz' ma'dum): A non-existent part; something that does not yet exist at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a buyer stipulates to leave fruit on the tree until ripening, and the fruit has stopped growing, the sale is:
A) Valid, because the fruit is complete
B) Fasid, because the condition is contrary to the requirement of the contract
C) Valid, by istihsan due to custom
D) Batil, because it is a sale of a non-existent thing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a condition to leave fruit on the tree renders the sale fasid (invalid) per the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), though Imam Muhammad allows it by istihsan when growth is complete; the mufta bihi is the fasid view.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Sale of Fruit with Condition to Leave on Tree: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on sale of fruit with condition to leave on tree, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the sale of fruit with a condition to leave it on the tree and apply the mufta bihi (Imam Muhammad's istihsan) to determine validity based on the fruit's growth stage.

Core Idea
A condition contrary to the contract invalidates a sale—unless custom overrides by istihsan.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a common agricultural transaction: buying fruit with the condition that it remains on the tree until ripening. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold such a condition invalidates the sale because it contradicts the contract's requirement of immediate delivery and involves a prohibited 'transaction within a transaction.' Imam Muhammad, however, deems it permissible by istihsan (juristic preference) when the fruit has reached full size, due to widespread custom. The relied-upon fatwa follows Imam Muhammad's view. We will walk through the proofs, the ikhtilaf, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
State the default Hanafi ruling for selling fruit with the condition to leave it on the tree. Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the sale fasid (voidable) because the condition contradicts muqtada al-'aqd (the contract's requirement) and also constitutes safqatani fi safqah (a transaction within a transaction), which is prohibited by hadith.
The hadith prohibition of safqatani fi safqah is a key proof.
2
Analyze the condition as contrary to the contract
Understand that the contract of sale inherently requires immediate delivery of the object. Stipulating that the fruit remain on the seller's trees means the buyer leaves his property in the seller's trees, occupying the seller's property. This is contrary to muqtada al-'aqd.
3
Analyze the condition as a transaction within a transaction
Leaving fruit on the tree is either for compensation (lease) or free (loan). Both are separate contracts. Adding one to the sale creates safqatani fi safqah, which is prohibited. However, note that this argument applies more clearly to land (crops) than to trees, since leasing/loaning trees may not be valid. The primary reason for invalidity in the fruit case remains the condition being contrary to the contract.
The commentator clarifies this nuance.
4
Recognize the exception for full-grown fruit
When the fruit has reached its full size (mutanahi al-'uzm) and no further growth is expected, Imam Muhammad allows the sale by istihsan due to custom. The two elders still consider it invalid. The fatwa is on Imam Muhammad's view.
Custom (ta'amul) is a valid basis for istihsan in the Hanafi school.
5
Distinguish the case of non-full-grown fruit
If the fruit is not yet full-grown, the sale is invalid by consensus because the condition involves a non-existent part (juz' ma'dum) that will come into existence after the contract from the seller's property. Selling existing and non-existing together is invalid.
This consensus includes all three imams.
